Exhausting the people—
Then it is like the tiger’s torn flesh,
The wind’s shattered roofs—
Is it not fitting?

How may this be made clear?

Zhuangzi said:
To eat when starving yet gluttonously,
To drink when thirsty yet excessively,
To plunge into water when scorched,
To rush into fire when freezing—
Though the suffering ends,
The body must die.

A chest’s obstruction cannot be drilled,
A throat’s ailment cannot be scraped,
A gnat’s sting cannot be shot,
A louse’s bite cannot be hacked—
Why?
To remove small woes
Is to invite greater ruin.

The wise sovereign and sagely ruler govern a great state
Like handling the finest threads,
Like guiding a tiny stream,
Like discerning the autumn hair,
Like hearing nothing yet all is heard—
As if present yet absent,
As if true yet false,
As if acting yet resting,
In the realm where action is both action and non-action,
In the wild where transformation is in both having and not having—
Where blessings are subtle and benefits scarce,
Where words are few and prohibitions rare,
Where movement occurs in the formless,
And achievements flow throughout the lands.

He who grasps the essentials,
Moves little yet gains much.
He who loses the opportunity,
Acts much yet gains little.
Thus, the subtle opportunity
Exceeds the expectations of ten thousand men—
As if in both having and not having,
It cannot be shown to others.

Why is this so?
Because he takes **Dao/Way** as father,
And **virtue/power** as mother,
Divine wisdom as teacher,
And **Great Harmony (太和)** as friend,
Clarity and stillness as constant,
Simplicity and ease as master,
Heaven and Earth as law,
And the interplay of **yin** and **yang** as model—
The sun and moon as ritual,
All things as exemplars,
Responding to circumstances as fundamental principle,
And sincerity as the foremost virtue.

Distinguishing roles and duties,
Guiding with subtle yet profound silence,
Drawing forth the threads of governance,
Lifting the great essentials while seeking them within himself—
Thus, the people are like embryos,
The ruler is like an infant,
Cunning and deceit have no cause to arise,
And cleverness finds no opportunity to emerge.
All things are equal and balanced,
None overflowing, none deficient,
In perfect accord and compliance,
All things flourish abundantly,
Spirits and humans unite,
Together thriving in harmony,
Acting naturally without constraint,
Each fulfilling its inherent potential.

Silent as if without a ruler,
Secure as if without spirits—
All things grow to completion,
The people live to their natural span,
Reaching the end of their days,
None perish prematurely.

This is called **Dao/Way** nurturing the world,
Its spirits not acting with malevolence.
The ruler, if not **virtue/power** is lacking,
The spirits, if not divine, are not malevolent.
The ruler is not lacking in **virtue/power**,
Yet he universally applies benevolence,
His **virtue/power** permeates all directions,
And he does not favor or pity selectively.

The spirits are not malevolent,
Yet they float in vague uncertainty,
They ride upon the slightest changes,
They traverse all lands,
Conforming to time’s transformations,
Their divine essence encompassing all things,
Yet they do not harm humanity.

It is not that their divine essence does not harm,
But when the sage rules above,
He aligns with Heaven,
Humans and spirits harmonize,
Great transformation flows naturally,
Cunning does not arise,
Strange things do not emerge,
None are full, none are empty—
The world murmurs in accord,
All things equal and balanced,
Neither raising high nor digging deep,
Not assaulting metals and stones,
Not blocking rivers and springs,
People do not migrate far,
Food is not scarce—
All may be enjoyed,
None can be shaped or forced,
Grasses and trees yellow and then fall,
Humans transform fully and then end.
Thus, the sage does not harm.

Therefore, what Heaven nurtures,
What Earth contains,
What **Great One (太一)** governs,
What **Heavenly Unity (天一)** leads—
The four seasons converge,
The five phases align,
Joy and sorrow share the same threshold,
Fortune and misfortune share the same gate.
Thus, what brings peace also brings peril,
And what brings peril also brings peace;
What preserves life also brings death,
And what brings death also preserves life.
He who knows peace yet forgets peril is secure,
He who knows security yet forgets peril is in danger.
How may this be made clear?

The tiger and leopard, with their majestic forms and broad adornments,
Firm claws and teeth, resplendent hides and abundant fur,
Distinct markings bright and clear—
Thus they seize all beasts yet are mastered by humankind.

The fragrant and splendid grasses, with their radiant hues and sweet scents,
Thus delight the multitude yet harm their own roots.

The sovereign of a great realm, with vast lands and teeming people,
High status and favored form, towering might and illustrious name—
Thus they provoke neighboring states yet endanger their own rule.

When the **Dao/Way** is upheld, they are steadfast as a flat stone,
Secure as the great mountain.

When the **Dao/Way** is lost, they are perilous as a pile of eggs,
Light as a feather.

Both weak, they suffer first;
Both chaotic, they perish first.
Thus, great realms are the steps to tyranny
And the stage to ruin.

Therefore, the sovereign of a great realm
Stands alone with none to rival,
Mountains encircle their borders, three sides blocked by defenses,
Rivers unite their flow, all things beneath them bow—
The hub of the feudal lords.

A storehouse of fertile lands,
The corpse of the strong and mighty,
The master of power and influence,
Bound by unmeasured steadfastness,
Choked by narrow and constrained paths.

Abundant and rich, vast and easy,
Spanning thousands of miles,
Treasure and marvels without end,
Few taxes or burdens upon the people,
Herds and flocks roam freely,
Boats and carriages numberless,
Arms and provisions piled high,
The people dwell in ease and joy,
Travelers pause and stay—
Men like grass and trees,
Cattle filling the hills and fields,
Hunting and tilling, their gains tenfold.

Thus, all under Heaven desire to submit,
And ministers and generals strive to attach—
With chariots and cavalry charging boldly,
A million armored men,
Defending easy terrain, holding strong passes,
Favored by Heaven’s order, Earth’s support—
They suspend life, dictate death,
Assist them and they gain strength,
Betray them and they lose their ancestral halls.
Thus, the feudal lords desire their alliance,
And all under Heaven fears their might.

So-called great realms,
Are the currents of all under Heaven,
The meeting of all under Heaven’s exchanges.

When the **Dao/Way** is extended, they can unite and encompass,
When virtue and favor are exalted, they can issue commands,
When they are struck, they can exalt benevolence,
When they govern, they can clarify righteousness,
When they unite, they can expand their lands,
When they take, they can grow their wealth—
Thus the feudal lords delight in them,
And ministers and generals profit from them.

Therefore, from ancient times to today,
Great realms—like the female—
Conquer with stillness the male,
Unchanged for ten thousand ages,
Unaltered for a hundred thousand years.

Why is this so?
They receive the full with emptiness,
They respond to the present with the void,
They do not seek to be great,
But strive to be small,
They do not seek to be high,
But always remain low.

The female guards with yielding softness,
The male seeks with forceful vigor—
Thus the female ever conquers the male with stillness.

Excessive fullness brings regret,
Hence great realms should act as the lowly.

Thus, the enlightened kings and sage rulers in great realms
Extend the **Dao/Way** yet do not claim possession,
Act humbly yet do not seek subservience—
Their lands enfold the realms of feudal lords,
Yet none dare oppose them;
Their virtue encompasses the strength of feudal lords,
Yet none dare resist them;
They bow in submission to align with others’ will,
Their loyalty, integrity, and sincerity lead their officers—
They divide lands east and west to attract the worthy and brilliant,
They diligently till and weave to amass wealth and resources,
They form shifting alliances to guard against unforeseen threats.

They open granaries and distribute wealth,
Nourish the aged and feed the orphaned,
Relieve the poor and aid the destitute,
Honor recluses in their caves.

They receive yet do not take,
They grant yet do not withhold—
Softness, simplicity, and ease,
Taking no action while in position.

Though feudal lords may be greedy and vile,
Resentful and haughty, relying on strength,
And though they dislike compliance and plot rebellion,
They are still subdued by the **Dao/Way**’s might and virtue’s weight,
By yielding stillness and humility—
Then they mend their ways, correct their errors,
Cede lands and offer treasures,
Submit their allegiance, even risk their lives,
And beg to be made vassals, border guards of their realm.

So-called great realms acting humbly toward small realms,
Are those who take small realms.

When they occupy small and weak realms,
They follow the **Dao/Way** in action,
Govern with moderation and care,
Wealth in boats and carriages,
Strength in arms and provisions,
Loyalty, sincerity, and honesty—
They value simplicity and honor tillage,
Tilling and weaving are their duties,
They do not tax the people,
Superior and inferior in harmony,
Affection like father and son,
The ruler as the heart within the body,
The people as its limbs—
Their realm unified in peace,
They can share life and death,
Superior and inferior in accord,
They can join with neighboring states in mutual trust.

Though the sovereigns of great realms
Bear the weight of many strong powers,
Hold sway over influence and authority,
Left with virtue and right with benevolence,
Their hearts like hungry tigers,
Their wrath like surging springs—
They do not delight in giving,
But always wish to devour others.

Yet if they gain the will of Heaven,
The favor of the people,
The trust of ministers and generals,
The aid of neighboring states,
And raise the unmeasured might of their enemies,
If they display their unfathomable power,
If they humble their bodies and exhaust their sincerity,
If they act with clear and tranquil purpose—
Then their spirits are moved, their minds swayed,
Their wrath shifts, their calamities depart,
And they become my strange allies.

Small realms acting humbly toward great realms,
Are those who take great realms.

Those who take great realms,
Are those who seize what others depend upon.

The superior who takes from others,
Their lands vast, their influence abundant,
Their virtue broad and their power heavy—
This is what others seek.

Great realms, acting without force,
Can secure this advantage.

But they must always unite with the lowly,
And thus gain what others possess.

The inferior who takes from others,
Their lands narrow, their people few,
Their power light and their virtue scarce—
This is what others easily seize.

Small realms, being light,
Are often taken by others.
Forced to submit,
They must not be greedy.

Thus, without battle they destroy another’s city,
Without assault they surrender another’s stronghold—
Their lands expand, their names grow eminent,
Their ancestral halls stand in glory,
Their deeds and virtues flow without end—
This is what great realms aspire to.

Great realms, if they but guard their yielding nature,
Can naturally hope for such benefits.

In alliance with great realms,
In close ties and bonds of kinship—
When disasters strike, great realms share their concern,
When neighboring states are in peril, great realms offer aid,
They bend beneath one man yet raise ten thousand above,
Their altars honored, their ancestral halls illustrious—
Their realm rich, their armies strong,
Their people and resources complete—
Their prosperity extends without end,
This is what small realms wish for.

Small realms serve the great with humility,
And thus the great themselves bear their concern—
This is how they secure their survival.

Hence, they border neighboring lands with measured restraint,
Hold the scales of power without swaying,
Act first to gain first advantage,
The humble govern the proud,
The still overcome the restless,
They occupy the high ground while yielding to the great—
Small yields to great, small gains from great.

Why is this so?
The Way of Nature cannot be forced.
Water flows downward when moved,
Humans act when drawn by gain.
If the great release their dominance and entrust matters,
The many weak unite as one,
Emerging from the unexpected—
This is how the strong perish.

Thus, the great should yield.
When the great oppress the small, calamity follows.
When the small retreat in humility, aid comes from the many—
Therefore, the great should embrace yielding.

---
*Daoist Canon: The Dao of the *Zhi Gui* (指归)
Volume Ten: On Calamities [Corrected from original "Non-Harm"]
Volume Eleven: The Child of the Valley [Annotated by Shùjūn Yán Zhūn, courtesy name Jūn Píng]*

---
*[Note: The following annotations reflect textual variants from the *Secret Register* (秘册本) and *Yìlán* (怡兰本) editions, as referenced in the original text.]*

---

**Chapter on the Essence of All Things**

The Dao/Way is the essence of all things, opening and guiding them.
It is the treasure of the virtuous, who embody **weakness** (*yì* 翕),
While the unvirtuous cannot preserve it—
They are bound by **rigid constraints** (*qūyuē* 拘约).

**Fine words** (*měiyán* 美言) can secure what one desires,
**Noble conduct** (*zūnxíng* 尊行) can elevate one to high rank.
Yet humans, when unvirtuous, love to act contrary—
Why should they abandon **favor** (*chǒng* 宠) and endure **deprivation** (*xiāo* 削)?

Thus, the **Sage-King** (*Shàngzì* 上子) establishes the **Son of Heaven** (*Tiānzǐ* 天子),
And sets the **Three Exalted Ministers** (*Sāngōng* 三公)—
All are **virtuous and sage** (*xiánshèng* 贤圣).
Even if one offers **a jade tablet** (*gǒngbì* 拱璧) as a light burden,
Or **four steeds** (*xiāndàmǎ* 先驷马) for secure dwelling,
It is not as precious as **this Dao**—
**Words and deeds** (*yánxíng* 言行) refined and cultivated.

The ancients valued this Dao because it harmonizes the human heart—
Why not say: *"Seek and gain"* (*qiúyǐdé* 求以得) is but **power and force** (*shìzé* 势则),
And *"Crime forgiven, none to blame"* (*yǒuzuìyǐmiǎn* 有罪以免) is mere **self-indulgence** (*wéiwéiyǐwéi* 无以为)?

Thus, this Dao is **held in highest esteem** (*guì* 贵) throughout the world.

**On Calamities** (*Zhǐguī* 指归):
Wood grows from its **root** (*běn* 本) to its **tip** (*mò* 末).
The tip depends on the **branches** (*tiáo* 条),
The branches on the **limbs** (*zhī* 枝),
The limbs on the **trunk** (*jìng* 茎),
The trunk on the **root** (*běn* 本),
The root on **Heaven and Earth** (*Tiāndì* 天地),
And Heaven and Earth receive it from the **formless** (*wúxíng* 无形).

**Flower and fruit** (*huāshí* 华实) arise from **vital qi** (*yǒuqì* 有气),
Vital qi from the **four seasons** (*sìshí* 四时),
The four seasons from **yin-yang** (*yīnyáng* 阴阳),
Yin-yang from Heaven and Earth,
And Heaven and Earth receive it from the **formless**.

Thus, I know the Dao/Way—
From the **formless shape** (*wúxíng* 无形) and **featureless aspect** (*wúzhuàng* 无状),
Opening **emptiness** (*xū* 虚),
Guiding **spiritual penetration** (*shéntōng* 神通),
Harmonizing Heaven and Earth,
Calming yin-yang,
Regulating the four seasons,
Resolving the myriad regions,
Diverse forms and kinds all flourish and complete.
**Transformation and change** (*biànhuà* 变化) know no end—
The **constant** (*cháng* 常) is **non-action** (*wúwéi* 无为).
No love nor hatred, it aligns with all things—
All classes respond, each attaining its proper course.

This is the Dao/Way, the essence of all things.
The virtuous who attain it **expand and contract** (*yìzhāng* 以翕以张),
Pure and serene, soft and weak,
Silent and indistinct—
**Ren** (*rén* 仁) is gentle and harmonious,
Nourishing and tranquil, **virtue** (*dé* 德) is **power** (*shì* 势) yet **subtle** (*wúxíng* 无形).
**Great and small** (*dàxiǎo* 大小) are **comprehensive** (*zhōumì* 周密),
Fine and minute are **invisible** (*wúxíng* 无形),
**Mysterious penetration** (*Xuándá* 玄达) reaches all affairs,
And returns to the **Nameless** (*wúmíng* 无名).

**Change and return** (*zhōngshǐ* 终始) are **reversed** (*fǎnfù* 反覆),
All blessings arise naturally—
Acting, one secures what is desired,
Stillness, one avoids what is harmful.
Above all others, its **virtue** (*wéi* 威) and **power** (*dé* 德) are **self-evident** (*zìmíng* 自明).
It **overcomes the firm** (*jīan* 坚) and **conquers the great** (*dà* 大),
None can rival it in **duality** (*shuāng* 双).
Thus, it is the treasure of the virtuous.

The **wicked** (*xiōng* 凶) who attain it **emit and expand** (*fāzhāng* 以发以张),
Firm and rigid, yet **coarse and scattered** (*yǐcū* 以疏),
**Substance and action** (*shí* 实) come **first** (*xiān* 先),
**Arrogance** (*jiāo* 骄) overflows, **swelling** (*yì* 溢) in **grandeur** (*zhuàngdà* 壮大),
**Fullness** (*shèng* 盛) and **excess** (*mǎn* 满) **strengthen to extremity** (*yǐqiángjí* 以强极),
**Ambitious cultivation** (*guāngxiū* 广修) is **boundless** (*wújiāng* 无疆),
**Bright scrutiny** (*zhāochá* 照察) is **dazzling** (*yǐngyǐng* 以荧荧),
**Clear display** (*xiǎndé* 显的) is **radiant** (*yǐzhāngzhāng* 以彰彰).

The **strong grow small** (*qiángdàzhōngxiǎo* 强大终小),
**Calamity does not come** (*bùhuòzìshēng* 不祸自生)—
Acting, one loses what is desired,
Stillness, one suffers harm.
**Heart troubled** (*xīn yōu* 忧), **resolve diminished** (*zhì xiāo* 志削),
Then one **returns to the norm** (*fǎnzhèngcháng* 反正常).

**Spiritual qi** (*shénqì* 神气) is **agitated** (*fáncù* 烦促),
**Contracting** (*qū* 趋) while **expanding** (*zhāng* 张) is abandoned,
**Oppressed and confined** (*yùyuē* 郁约), yet **words are humble** (*cí bēi* 辞卑),
**Bound and restrained** (*jūzhì* 拘制), yet **body is lowered** (*tǐ jiàng* 体降),
**Driven to peril** (*pòxiǎn* 迫险), yet **guests are subservient** (*bīn fú* 宾伏),
**Anxious and fearful** (*wěidá* 悿怛), yet **loyal and trustworthy** (*zhōngxìn* 忠信),
**Altering appearance** (*gǎiyǒng* 改容) while **easing restraints** (*yìjié* 易节),
**Aligning with the virtuous** (*yǔjūnzǐ* 与君子同).

**Crime is fixed** (*zuìdìng* 罪定) yet **words are good** (*yánshàn* 而言善),
**At death’s door** (*línsǐ* 临死) yet **love for life** (*ài shēn* 爱身),
**Obedient to Heaven’s decree** (*yīfèng Tiānshù* 一奉天数),
**Changing nature** (*biànxìng* 变性) and **easing emotions** (*yìqíng* 易情),
**Finding joy in poverty** (*ān pín* 安贫) and **delighting in hardship** (*lè kùn* 乐困),
**Lowliness and humiliation** (*bēijiàn* 卑贱) as **constant** (*cháng* 常),
**Honoring Heaven** (*zūn Tiān* 尊天) and **respecting spirits** (*jìng guǐ* 敬鬼),
**Viewing others as kings** (*shì rén rú wáng* 视人如王),
**Above like cattle and horses** (*shàng bǐ niúmǎ* 上比牛马),
**Below like dogs and sheep** (*xià liè quǎnyáng* 下列犬羊),
**Heaven’s net is drawn** (*Tiānwǎng yǐfā* 天网以发),
**Yet nature does not hear** (*zìrán bùtīng* 自然不听).

Ah, how painful it is!
This warning is **most clear** (*shènmíng* 甚明).
The unvirtuous do not preserve themselves—
They **burden** (*lěi* 累) the **True** (*Zhēn* 真).
When the burden is complete,
They **regret too late** (*fānghuǐ bùjí* 方悔不及).

This is like **digging a well when thirsty** (*línkě juéjǐng* 临渴掘井),
Not as wise as **ordering affairs before chaos arises** (*lǐ yú wèiluàn* 理於未乱).
These two paths diverge—
Both stem from **Daoic practice** (*dàoxíng* 道行),
**One precedes, one follows** (*zàiqián zàihòu* 在前在后),
**One thrives, one perishes** (*huòcún huòwáng* 或存或亡).
The virtuous **practice the Dao first** (*xiāndào* 先道) and **thrive**,
The wicked **practice the Dao late** (*hòudào* 后道) and **perish**.

Thus, **words and deeds** are the **litigation of the self** (*zhìshēn zhī yù* 治身之狱).
**Timely harmony** (*shíhé* 时和) and **order of precedence** (*xiānhòu* 先后)
Belong to the **Great Destiny** (*dàmìng* 大命).

Therefore, the **virtuous person** (*jūnzǐ* 君子) in **self-cultivation** (*lìshēn* 立身):
**Like the deaf** (*rú hā* 如哈) and **like the mute** (*rú lóng* 如聋),
**Like the uncarved block** (*ruò pǔ* 若朴) or **plain wood** (*ruò zhì* 若质),
**Hiding words in the heart** (*cángyán yúxīn* 藏言於心),
**Ever dwelling in silent mystery** (*chángchǔ xuánmò* 常处玄默),
When speaking, **deep reflection** (*shēn sī* 深思),
**Voice rises like weeping** (*fāshēng ruòkū* 发声若哭),
**Harmonious and yielding** (*héshùn shíshì* 和顺时适),
**Completing others’ blessings** (*chéng rén zhī fú* 成人之福),
**Responding with modest refusal** (*yìngduì cí ràng* 应对辞让),
**Straight yet undecorated** (*zhí ér bùshì* 直而不饰).

Thus, **speech fills the world** (*yán mǎn tiānxìa* 言满天下) yet **is not excessive** (*bù duō* 不多),
**Vibrations reach the four seas** (*zhèndòng sìhǎi* 振动四海) yet **are not hasty** (*bù sù* 不速),
**Connecting all things** (*liánjiē wànwù* 连接万物) yet **not possessing** (*bù yǒu* 不有),
**Words move the world** (*cí dòng tiānxià* 辞动天下),
Each attaining what they desire.

**Fine words** can be **traded** (*shì* 市).
In **governing the world** (*jīngshì* 经世),
Their **qi and resolve** (*qìzhì* 气志) are **obscured** (*yǎomíng* 窅冥),
Their **form and bearing** (*xíngróng* 形容) are **hidden** (*yǐn* 隐),
**Dwelling as if in fear** (*jū rú jīngkǒng* 居如惊恐),
**Appearance seems insufficient** (*mào sì bùzú* 貌似不足),
**Diminished and faint** (*pí pí chǎ chǎ* 偆偆漻漻),
**Dissolving like ice melting** (*xiāo rú bīngjiě* 消如冰释),
**Extending with the times** (*yù shí ér shēn* 遇时而伸),
**Hiding when the world is unready** (*zāo shì ér fú* 遭世而伏),
**Sharing Heaven’s sorrow** (*yǔ Tiān tóng yōu* 与天同忧),
**Heart deeply moved** (*zhōngxīn rě* 中心恻恻),
**Plans fail to execute** (*jìhuà bùxíng* 计画不行),
**Adapting to the times** (*suí shí fǎncè* 随时反侧),
**Humility and modesty** (*qiánxū zhǐzú* 谦虚止足),
**Self-abasing** (*bēisǔn zìmù* 卑损自牧).

**Joy in lowliness** (*lè xià* 乐下) like water,
Enduring yet **never rigid** (*jiǔ ér bùshì* 久而不式),
**Lowering ever further** (*xià zhī yòu xià zhī* 下之又下之),
To the **ultimate extreme** (*yuánjí* 元极),
The world responds to them—
Thus, they can **have a realm** (*yǒu guó* 有国).

**Noble conduct** can **elevate others** (*jiā rén* 加人).
Why is this so?

「Human nature and disposition are such that they delight in honor and favor, abhor lowliness and shame, resent when diminished, rejoice when enriched, take pleasure in being lowered, and scorn being restrained. This is the universal Dao of past and present, and the natural logic of human hearts.

The worthy practice this even more so; the petty even more so. Therefore, when one cultivates noble speech and conduct, there will be no misfortune in one’s undertakings.

In ancient times, those who led the people—how could they be abandoned? The officials of Jie and Zhou could be made to follow virtue. The soldiers of Qin and Chu could be made to follow virtue.

What is called "what people do not approve of"—how could it be abandoned?

Thus, if one can win their hearts, the realm may be obtained; if one cannot grasp their intentions, even one’s wives and concubines will not obey.

How can this be demonstrated?

The rank of nobility commands the realm,
Wealth possesses the four seas,
Might and influence are boundless,
Absolute power and authority are held—
these are what people fear.

To establish the Son of Heaven.

To depart from the ranks of foot soldiers,
To leave the common troops,
To rise with grand purpose like a dragon,
To assist the Son of Heaven,
To spread the Way and display virtue,
To enact all good governance,
To let benevolence flow to all households,
To honor and illuminate one’s ancestors—
these are what people benefit from.

To appoint the Three Exalted Ministers.

Using what people fear to seek what they benefit from,
With speech that is not beautiful and conduct that is not respectful,
Even if one presents the great jade tablet or holds precious treasures to offer,
Even if one rides in a fine carriage with four horses,
The worthy’s heart will remain doubtful and hesitant,
The Mysterious Sage will remain deeply hidden,
And the gentleman will not come.

Because the Son of Heaven possesses an awe-inspiring might,
And the Three Exalted Ministers bestow abundant blessings upon the people,
Yet relying solely on oneself to lord over others,
Offering the jade and silk of favors,
People will not submit to it.

Thus, it is said: *It is better to sit and advance this Dao than to labor with carriages and treasures.*

When speech and conduct are refined within,
Then the spirit and qi overflow outward.
Without the toil of four horses or the expense of precious jades,
The scholars of the land will respond in harmony like the wind,
And the virtuous, heroic, and talented will gather from all directions,
Their radiance reaching its peak.
The Sage will descend, and the court will abound with gentlemen.

This is because of *sitting and advancing this Dao*.

Why did the ancients value this Dao so highly?
Because speech aligns with Heaven and Earth yet does not boast,
Conduct harmonizes with human hearts yet does not rely on force,
Reputation is achieved without display,
Accomplishment is realized without possession.

Emotion and disposition flow naturally,
Not seized for personal gain,
But to follow the Way,
Not for oneself,
All things return to it,
And it governs the realm.

This is called *obtaining without seeking, having virtue without claiming*—
Thus, it is valued by all under Heaven.

**On the Chapter of Non-Action (Wuwei)**

Non-action is formless,
Non-doing is silent,
Tastelessness is nameless.

Great or small, much or little—virtue and power are manifest,
Yet repaying grudges with virtue is done before they arise.
Planning for difficulties in ease,
Considering what is not yet formed,
Acting for the great in the small,
Making the subtle profound—
This is how the Sage says that even the brightest ruler
Never undertakes great matters in their slightest form,
Thus achieving their grandest purpose and aligning with humanity.

Those who make light of promises must be few in trust—*this cannot be done*.
Those who act on many trifles must face many calamities—*this cannot be relied upon*.

Even the Sage finds difficulty in constant scheming,
Thus ultimately avoiding calamity and achieving completeness.

**On the Principle of Return (Zhigui):**

The numbers of the Divine Mind,
The Dao of Nature—
What is not born does not perish,
What is born does not persist,
What neither perishes nor persists
Is the birth of what neither perishes nor persists.

From this perspective,
Sorrow does not generate sorrow,
Joy does not generate joy,
Neither sorrow nor joy
Is the birth of sorrow and joy.

Thus, those dwelling in misfortune gain blessings,
Those dwelling in blessings suffer misfortune.
The master of blessings and misfortune lies with the ruler.

To act without form,
To listen without sound,
Without form or sound, it becomes profound and far-reaching—
Thus, *non-action’s function is great*, while *action’s function is small*,
*Virtue’s power is thin*, while *non-virtue’s power is thick*.

One who embodies *neither non-being nor being* yet generates *non-being and being*
Can transform misfortune and blessings,
Neither misfortune nor blessing,
Yet can resolve both.

Like a mirror before one’s face,
Acting before what has not yet arisen—
Thus, it becomes profound and far-reaching, great and thick.

Hence, the Sage does not act for being,
Does not act for non-being,
Does not act for death,
Does not act for life,
But wanders in the realm of *non-being*,
Dwells in the space between death and life,
Adapting and responding naturally,
Thus maintaining the constant.

Therefore, *without looking, they see*,
*Without listening, they hear*,
Supporting and stabilizing Heaven and Earth,
Embellishing the Way and nourishing the spirit,
Lifting and guiding all things,
Ruling the imperial realm and governing the people,
Freeing emotions and releasing intentions—
All return to their original truth.

This is called *Non-action as Non-action*—
Simply responding to the appropriate changes.

They have never truly known,
Thus they can do without limit.

Not creating life’s burdens,
Not stirring up affairs,
Not adding with kindness,
Not bestowing with benefit,
Teaching what cannot be taught,
Guiding what lacks awareness,
Cutting off what the people delight in,
To follow their true emotions,
Permitting what the people abhor,
To grasp their true intentions.

Exiting the door of untimely death,
Entering the path of eternal life,
Soaring in the Mysterious Depths,
Rejoicing in the Great Primordial,
Dim and vast, beyond knowing,
Thick and murky, as if in darkness—
The world knows no troubles.

This is called *Non-doing as doing*—
Simply altering what has already become.

They have never truly thought,
Thus they reach *Non-doing*.

Tasting what is tasteless,
Observing what is formless—
Thus they can distinguish the categories of similarity and difference,
And clarify the nature of right and wrong.

Acting before what has not yet arisen,
Fixing before it has tilted—
Brave deeds are unseen,
Wise names are unclaimed,
Blessings do not arise,
Misfortunes do not take root—
*Blessings without blessings*
Rest in the silent,
*Misfortunes without misfortunes*
Cease in the nameless,
Ensuring the people’s peace and joy,
And the realm’s great harmony.

This is called *Tastelessness as taste*—
Simply discerning what has not yet sprouted.

They have never truly intended,
Thus they achieve without limit.

Therefore, the ruler worries and alleviates calamity,
Extends and secures blessings,
Kills traitors who dishonor the lord,
Avenges those who insult the father,
Overcomes those who create difficulties,
Destroys those who stir up chaos,
Repels bandits who invite disaster,
Punishes rebels who defy order.

When brave deeds are seen, they conflict with Heaven,
When wise names arise, they oppose the Way.」

「When actions stir the people’s delight,
Yet labor yields little virtue—
When deeds are done and the people rejoice,
Yet the giver’s kindness is slight.

If one cannot practice *wuwei* (無為) as *wuwei*,
And treat affairs as nonexistent,
Then the vast benefits of earlier efforts
Are outweighed by the petty gains of later kindness—
Great and small differ in measure,
And blame for calamity is unavoidable.
Thus it is said: *"Great and small, much and little."*

Therefore, loyalty and trust are undermined,
Righteous undertakings perish,
And those who oppose naturally submit,
While the clever fly high.

Those who usurp merit are exposed,
Those who steal authority are revealed,
The violent prosper in chaos,
The deceitful thrive in corruption.

Right and wrong are inverted,
The realm teeters toward collapse,
Things lose their natural order,
And states perish in ruin.

Thus, it is better to prevent calamity
Than to remedy it after it has grown great;
To act before disorder arises
Is nobler than to speak after it has begun.

This is the benefit of *unspeaking*,
The nobility of *wuwei*.

Hence, the virtuous one
Acts before the first stirrings,
Stays still in the absence of absence,
Spreads the Dao and bestows virtue,
Transforms and harmonizes in the mysterious,
Angers without showing anger,
Speaks without uttering words,
Strikes without daring to attack,
Defends without a fixed end,
Commands without force,
And avenges grudges before they sprout.

*Virtue* is the root of order,
*Grudges* are the end of affairs.
To extinguish the end and return to the root,
One must cultivate virtue and revert to the true—
Then affairs and things dissolve unseen before they manifest.

Thus it is said:
To act before the first stirrings,
To speak before speaking—
Is to avenge grudges with virtue.

Plotting against difficulties while they are easy,
Treating their fundamental causes,
Eradicating them before signs appear,
So they cannot take form—
Then deeds are as fine as autumn hair,
And achievements as vast as Tai Mountain.

Greatness over smallness,
Tending to things thread by thread,
Respectful and cautious,
As if beginning anew each time,
Not yielding to desires,
Not seeking personal advantage,
Always opposing what is done,
Alone preserving the origin—
With time, all benefits accumulate,
Daily progress knows no bounds.
Though one does not strive for greatness,
Affairs unfold naturally.

This is plotting against difficulties while they are easy,
Yet greatness arises from smallness.

Therefore, when great calamities are about to emerge,
They are like evil winds invading a person.
Before they manifest,
Guard against them so they do not come.
When they lodge in the skin and hair,
Use warm compresses to dispel them.
When they reach the joints and tendons,
Apply gentle admonitions to remove them.
When they affect the internal organs,
A hundred medicines can cure them.
When they penetrate to the marrow,
Even Heaven and Earth cannot alleviate them,
And *zaohua* (造化) cannot treat them.

This is what is meant by *"difficult matters arise from the easy."*

When great undertakings are about to begin,
They are like water flowing from mountains.
At first, it dampens and moistens,
Then ripples appear,
Steadily trickling and gathering,
It becomes rivers and valleys,
Swelling and surging,
Capable of carrying ships and floating stones,
And finally forming vast seas,
Deep and immeasurable.

This is how great undertakings arise from small beginnings.

Thus, when the sage establishes merit and renown,
Even minute beginnings can become manifest,
Even hidden origins can become clear,
Small beginnings can grow vast,
And concealed truths can become evident—
Their ambition lies beneath the myriad people,
So they become rulers and kings.

Their authority shakes the world,
The Four Seas are all rectified,
Their commands are obeyed, their governance accepted,
None can stand against them—
Their virtue aligns with Heaven and Earth,
Their wisdom shines like the sun and moon.

The sage never seeks greatness,
And thus achieves it.

Therefore, those who speak much of gratitude
Will be burdened with many affairs and still lack trust—
Such behavior cannot be sustained.
Those whose hearts easily sway
Will accumulate difficulties and change—
Such behavior cannot be relied upon.
Light promises lead to few fulfillments.

Trust, when easily given, leads to many calamities—
This is the natural order.

Thus, the sage remains inwardly silent,
Unmoved in action, speechless in words,
Unseeing in gaze, unhearing in listening.
In motion, they are like Heaven and Earth,
In stillness, like spirits and gods—
They achieve without doing,
They inspire trust without speaking,
Advance with no rivals,
Retreat with no limits,
Their body knows no minor worries,
Their state no trivial calamities.

The sage embodies the Dao,
Does not engage in artificial deeds,
Aligns with the true and cultivates virtue,
And naturally faces no calamities—
Thus they never encounter difficulties.

Why is this so?
Peril lies in what seems not perilous,
Ruination lies in what seems not ruined—
It is clearly visible alone,
And operates through formlessness.

In times of no danger,
They always regard it as dangerous;
In times of no ruin,
They always regard it as ruinous—
With trembling vigilance,
They act before the first signs appear,
Thus avoiding peril and ruin.

Their ease in holding fast is like their ease in maintaining the state and self,
Their ease in plotting against calamities is like their ease in preventing disasters before they arise,
Their ease in breaking calamities is like their ease in dispersing them before they settle,
Their ease in treating calamities is like their ease in transforming their roots before they manifest.

Those who act invite defeat,
Those who grasp invite loss—
Thus the sage, with *wuwei*, faces no defeat,
Knowing it comes from itself;
With *wuwei* in grasping, faces no loss,
Knowing virtue comes from itself.

When the people engage in affairs,
They do so with purpose—
The sage always stops them
Before their undertakings are fully formed,
Thus avoiding defeat and blame.

Therefore, the sage says of enlightened rulers:
Desiring what they do not desire,
Thinking what they have not thought—
They do not prize difficult-to-obtain treasures,
Nor value jade and pearls.

Learning what they have not learned,
Doing what is empty—
They restore what others have already passed,
And do not cling to the fading.

They aid the natural unfolding of all things,
Assisting Heaven and Earth,
Yet do not dare to act artificially,
Nor to uphold false traditions.

*Pointing to the origin:*
Before illness takes hold in a person,
It is easy for a physician to act;
Before a state faces peril,
It is easy for counselors to devise strategies;
Before grudges sprout like teeth,
It is easy to address them;
Before minor calamities grow strong,
It is easy to relieve them.

How should this be applied?»

Zhuangzi said:
When a cart’s wheel is still intact,
A child can guide it along—
Yet when it begins to wobble,
Toppling into ravines and heights,
A thousand men cannot steady it.

Before an egg is cracked,
A single finger can brush it—
Yet once it becomes a soaring wild goose,
Flapping its wings through the clouds,
No snare or net can ensnare it.

When a calf is still nursing,
A single rope can restrain it—
Yet once it becomes a bull,
No net can contain it.

When a tiger seizes the herd,
Feeding on oxen and horses,
No sword or spear can resist it.

Thus, a single drop of water,
Over time, becomes a river and sea;
A small serpent, if not killed,
Transforms into a divine dragon.

Accumulated goodness leads to auspicious fortune,
Unchecked evil leads to ruin.

Therefore, when a successor-ruler governs,
With no grievances or malice,
Ministers and officials are harmonized,
The people submit in peace,
Neighboring states trade freely,
No slanderers or traitors exist,
The realm is tranquil and undisturbed,
Superior and inferior in accord.
At such times,
Rule requires no coercion,
Governance demands no force—
Non-action, non-interference,
And none can overcome it.
Its stability is effortless to maintain.

Yet when a state teeters toward peril,
The people face ruin,
Disasters loom though not yet manifest—
At this juncture,
Safety rests within oneself, not others.
Counsel need not come from sages,
Strength need not come from warriors—
Yet even on one’s deathbed,
Disaster may already be sealed.
Its onset is easy to avert.

When the ruler abandons the Dao,
Ministers harbor resentment,
Neighboring states cease trade,
Officials grow negligent,
Disasters take root—
Though still weak and fragile.
At this stage,
Wise men plot in secret,
Yet before action is taken, peril arises,
Before force is applied, ruin shatters.
Its fragility is easy to break.

When the ruler strays from rectitude,
Ministers err in counsel,
Neighboring states harbor grudges,
The people waver,
Disasters have already surfaced—
Roots and branches unmoored,
Ambition rises, yet lacks support,
Alliances form but do not bind.
At this point,
Honoring the worthy while suppressing the masses,
Severing loyalty and resolve,
Listening to whispers and probing faults,
Seeking blame within oneself—
Yet even then, advisors scatter before battle is joined.
Its subtlety is easy to disperse,
Though already far harder than when unmanifest.

Thus, fortune and calamity arise from the nameless,
Life and death emerge from the subtle.
These two forces intertwine,
Shifting like winds and waves,
Extending and contracting like tides—
Advancing and retreating in chaos.
To hear them is impossible,
To see them is invisible,
Cunning cannot act upon them,
Wisdom cannot discern them.
The mechanisms of life and death, fortune and ruin,
Are subtle, chaotic, and unseen.

Therefore, the sage transforms through the Dao,
Teaches through personal example,
Acts before things take form,
Govern before disorder arises—
Without intervention, things settle naturally,
Without nurturing, things preserve themselves.
Action aligns with fortune,
Stillness diverges from ruin,
Mysterious and obscure—
None perceive its origin.

Preventing chaos before it arises,
Rectifying before imbalance,
Cutting off deceit at its root,
Suppressing falsehood at its source,
Sealing the door to vice,
Blocking the path to wrongdoing—
Before sprouts take hold,
Before signs appear,
Before destruction is visible,
Before annihilation is complete—
Teach without teaching,
Guide without naming,
Know without knowing,
Shape without form—
Chaos cannot take root,
Disorder cannot emerge,
The world governs itself,
Life unfolds naturally.

This is to act before existence,
To govern before chaos.

As for the great tree of Tai Mountain,
Its roots rest in the shade,
Its branches reach the sun—
Branches sprawl, leaves spread,
Fruit lush and green,
Trunks wide enough to embrace,
Towers high beyond measure—
Yet it grows from nothingness,
Completes itself through non-action.

What is called an embrace-worthy tree
Arises from the tiniest sprout.
A nine-tiered tower,
Broad as hills, towering a hundred *ren* high,
Piercing the heavens—
Yet it begins with a single clod of earth,
Rises from the ground.

Thus, the great is not forced to be great,
The small is not forced to be small,
The high is not forced to ascend,
The low is not forced to descend.
Neither great nor small—
Then greatness and smallness emerge.
Neither high nor low—
Then height and depth manifest.

Therefore, the artificer
Does not leave traces of his craft,
The non-artificer
Provides the path for creation.
Not great, not small—
This is the path of non-action.
Not high, not low—
This is the path of non-artificer.
Yet greatness and smallness,
Highness and lowness—
These are the traces of action,
The marks of the artificer.

Thus, what is completed is ruined,
What is pursued becomes harm,
What is clung to leads to death,
What is fostered is abandoned.
Grasping what one desires,
One loses it.
Clinging to what one thinks,
One loses it.
Holding onto oneself,
One loses oneself.
Fixating on one’s spirit,
One loses it.

Seeking the path of non-action
Yet clinging to traces of existence—
This is why the artificer brings ruin,
The grasper invites loss.

Therefore, the sage acts without acting,
Generates all things,
Does not cling, yet directs desire—
Like two craftsmen building a high tower,
Or Heaven and Earth nurturing mighty trees,
Naturally, without effort.
Non-action brings no ruin,
Non-clinging brings no loss.

Virtue and power do not envy,
Divine clarity does not harm,
Harmony knows no obstruction,
Greatness knows no defeat.
Life and death follow their course,
Fortune and misfortune find their due.
Those who align with the Way attain it,
Those who stray from it suffer it.

When people face ruin,
It is because they abandon spontaneity
And rely on cleverness and strength,
Rejecting their nature while embracing their undoing.

Why is this so?
Because they seek what they seek,
Desire what they desire.

Men’s undertakings often falter at completion
Because they cling to desire and lose their foundation.

If one could truly
Treasure the end as the beginning,
Act without desiring,
Guard what is not to be occupied,
Move before the origin,
Return before the start—
Acting as if not acting,
Having as if not having,
Though seemingly unaccomplished,
Things naturally align.

The beginning may stray from the Way,
Yet the end will never lose it—
Thus there is no failure.

When the spirit is disturbed and the essence muddied,
When clarity fails to reach and action misses its mark,
When stillness loses what it seeks—
This is the work of wealth and learning.

Only those who refine their emotions and transform their nature,
Who delight in desire without desire,
Who find joy in action without action—
These are the Way and virtue.

Wealth agitates the spirit,
Learning breeds deceit.
If one desires not to desire, wealth loses its power;
If one learns not to learn, daily loss returns to truth—
Thus aligning with the Dao and virtue,
No multitude of faults can persist.

Therefore, cherish the Way as one cherishes a beloved,
Abhor wealth as one abhors losing one’s own body,
Ponder thought beyond thought,
Seek what is beyond seeking—
Clear and all-encompassing,
Learning what cannot be known,
Skilled in shaping all things,
Yet knowing what cannot be done—
Turning from what the masses pursue,
Returning to the void and the formless.

Desiring not to desire, one enters the boundless mystery;
Learning not to learn, one reaches the utmost subtlety—
Penetrating what the enlightened cannot fathom,
Exhausting what the common cannot measure,
Achieving what the accomplished cannot do,
Attaining what none can possess.

The mind profound, the form serene,
Lying like a corpse, standing like withered wood—
No thought, no deliberation,
As if unaware of all things.

Let things follow their nature,
Let affairs unfold of themselves—
Empty, still, and solitary,
The body uninvolved,
All things in their proper place—
Towering like a mound, vast like the ocean’s expanse,
Raised and discarded, ever contrary to the world—
All things respond in kind,
Thus attaining depth and distance.

The world’s great reversal,
Moving with the divine transformation,
Aiding Heaven, assisting Earth,
Daring not to create what is good.

The true practitioner of the Way is manifest.

The ancients who excelled in the Way
Governed themselves thus—
Not to enlighten the people with knowledge,
But to blind them, sealing their hearts.

The people’s difficulty in governance cannot be measured,
For their wisdom abandons loyalty and sincerity.

To rule with wisdom benefits the state but harms the people;
Not to rule with wisdom preserves the natural purity—
The state’s blessing, the people’s survival.

Knowing these two paths—calamity and blessing—
These are the fundamental patterns of right and wrong.

Always knowing the patterns to apply to the ruler’s body—
This is called the profound virtue, unseen and unheard.

Profound virtue is deep, unseen,
Far, unimaginable,
Contrary to things, distinct from the spirit—
Until it reaches the perfect harmony of the world.

**The Conclusion:**
Dao and virtue, divine and mundane,
Pure and impure, the Great Harmony—
Heaven, Earth, and all things,
Like roots and branches.

Numbers follow in sequence,
Qi transformations connect—
Success and decline, fortune and misfortune,
All share in the same fate.

The intent of Dao and virtue,
The heart of Heaven and Earth—
Peaceful generation, joyful repose,
Hatred and injury, life and death—
Thus the Sage is named King of all things.

Benefiting others brings their blessing,
Harming them brings their fear—
The Sage fears greatly,
Fearful of future calamity,
Deeply tracing its origins—
None better than returning to nature,
For nature’s path lies in the formless.

How may this be made clear?
Zhuangzi said:
Heaven and Earth do not know the workings of virtue,
Yet they act accordingly;
All things do not see Heaven and Earth’s methods,
Yet they cannot be preserved;
The masses do not recognize the ruler’s intentions,
Yet they may be at ease;
The limbs and orifices do not understand the heart’s guidance,
Yet they remain whole.

The ruler of all things is like the heart to the body.
The heart to the body—
What comes first or last?
Flowing through the blood and vessels,
Present everywhere,
Above and below, inside and out—
Nothing escapes its influence.
Moving with change, containing the spirit,
Balanced within, contrary without—
Thus all is preserved.

Therefore, the ancient emperors and kings
Followed Dao and virtue,
Organized divine and mundane,
Unified purity and impurity,
Led the Great Harmony—
Not with innate knowledge to act,
Not to open the world or guide customs,
But to dull the people’s senses,
Seal their hearts,
Prevent them from knowing,
Return them to nature.

The ancients who excelled in the Way
Did not enlighten the people,
But sought to blind them.

Thus, placing the people in the bright and clear,
While dwelling in the murky and obscure,
Teaching them ignorance,
Guiding them with the formless—
Filial piety and brotherly love unseen,
Ren and righteousness unexalted,
The ruler without honor,
The wise without renown.

Teaching without teaching,
Flowing through the four seas,
Doing without doing,
Reaching the eight directions—
Moving with Heaven and Earth in rhythm,
Resting with Dao and virtue in harmony,
All things flourishing together,
Each knowing its place—
Names and realities arising together,
Each fulfilling its role.

Harmonious qi flows freely,
The world in childlike simplicity—
No knowledge, no desire,
No action, no achievement—
The heart like wood and soil,
The will like dead ashes—
Seeing no difference, no fortune or misfortune,
Thus the people are easily governed and the world may be at peace.

Therefore, what benefits the people is their ease,
What sustains them is their life—
Yet the people stray from ease and life, making governance difficult,
Because of their knowing.

When the people know, they desire to live;
Desiring to live, they begin their pursuits;
Pursuits begun, fame and gain arise;
Fame and gain arise, contention and strife follow;
Strife and contention breed great deceit;
Great deceit breeds difficulty in governance.

The people’s difficulty in governance stems from their excess of knowledge.

Thus, ruling with knowledge,
The world spawns cleverness, deceit, and artifice,
Strange things multiply,
Desires know no end,
Luxury and licentiousness cease not,
Deceit and perversion flourish,
The bold and the cunning vie for dominance,
Names proliferate in the valleys—
Great calamity arises.

Ministers deceive their lords,
Sons rebel against their fathers,
White is called black,
Absence is called presence,
Names and realities invert,
Banditry and slaughter ensue.

Unsettled and restless, none find peace,
Light and reckless, none remain whole—
Armies march lightly, the world turns to war,
The dead numberless, rivers run with blood.

Grief and resentment stir the heavens,
Stars scatter, sun and moon dim,
Yin and yang lose order,
All things are harmed—
Mountains wither, valleys dry,
Thousands of miles of barren earth,
The world in ruin,
Until people eat one another.

The fault lies not with Heaven,
For the Sage above delights in wisdom and instructs the myriad people.
When the excess of favoring wisdom reaches this point,
It is said that governing with wisdom is the ruin of the state—
A truth that cannot be false.

Abandon cleverness and artifice,
Let virtue be pure and simple.
Know alone, ponder alone,
Desire nothing seen.
Follow the people’s heart,
Close their ears and eyes to falsehoods.
Eat no five flavors,
Wear no five colors.
The ruler, like Heaven and Earth,
The people, like grasses and trees.
Dwell in rocky retreats, find peace in valleys,
Drink water, eat grass, seek no grains.
Know mother, recognize father,
Yet see no kin.
Stuporous and unknowing,
Unaware of east or west,
Men and women take no pleasure in one another,
Fathers and sons feel no fondness.
Wood and stone are not despised,
Gold and jade are not prized.
Clusters live together in harmony,
The world unites in one mind,
The eight extremes share purpose,
The nine regions align in wind.
Borer-beetles do not act,
Poisonous beasts do not arise,
Spiritual dragons dwell among people,
Qilin and phoenixes roam the courts.

Gentle winds blow,
Sweet springs trickle,
Sweet dew falls,
Red grasses flourish,
Fine millet thrives lush,
All things live eternally.

This is not Heaven’s blessing,
But the ruler’s knowing of the Unknowable,
Naming the Unnameable.
The Unknowing-Knowing,
The Unnameable-Name,
Aligns with the Dao—
How could it be otherwise?

Thus, dimmed wisdom recognizes stupidity,
And aligns with the Dao’s essence.
Knowing stupidity, knowing wisdom,
Both align with the Dao’s intent.
Governance follows this,
Sometimes bringing harm, sometimes benefit.
To discern harm and benefit,
Great harmony naturally arises.
To grasp benefit and harm,
All things naturally align.

To govern with wisdom brings calamity and precedent;
To govern without wisdom brings blessing and virtue.
These two paths serve as models.
Ever knowing these models,
This is called *Xuan De* (玄德).

Thus, the recognition of stupidity and wisdom
Overcomes all obstacles.
In purity and stillness, a tranquil foundation,
A hidden blessing for all beings,
Unseen by the world—
Thus it is called *Xuan De*.

*Xuan De* is profound, immeasurable;
Distant, beyond reach;
Opposed to things, none can overcome it.
It is said: *Xuan De* is profound, distant, opposed to things.
When *Xuan De* descends,
It drifts, surges, retreats, indistinct—
Opposing things in action,
Harmonizing with virtue and the Way,
Guiding spirit and clarity,
Embracing all lands,
Unbounded in scope.
Beyond the six extremes, within the minutest measure,
No place is untouched by its virtue and benevolence,
Preserving life and nature,
Ending at Heaven’s appointed time,
Nurturing what is natural.
This is called *Great Harmony*.

**The Chapter on Rivers and Seas**
Rivers and seas become kings of all valleys
Because they are thus—
They are below all streams,
Thus they rule all valleys without submission.

Therefore, the Sage says of the enlightened ruler:
To place oneself above the people is to occupy the highest position,
Yet to speak in humility aligns with the people’s hearts.
To act before the people is to avoid their calamities,
Yet to place oneself last ensures their security.
To lead the people is to be their model,
Yet to harm none clarifies one’s virtue.
The world willingly follows,
Yet the ruler does not know he is being offered—
Not to compete for position,
Thus none can contend, none can prevail.

**The Principle of Return:**
Virtue and the Way do not create the myriad things,
Yet the myriad things arise naturally.
Heaven and Earth do not gather the myriad beings,
Yet the myriad beings find refuge in them.
Natural things do not seek to rule,
Yet things rule themselves.

Thus, Heaven and Earth, vast and myriad, are ruled by the Dao;
The myriad suns blazing, are ruled by Heaven;
The yin essence flowing, is ruled by Earth;
The naked dwell in caves, ruled by the Sage;
The winged soar in emptiness, ruled by the Divine Phoenix;
The furred tread the earth, ruled by the Qilin;
The scaled dwell in waters, ruled by the Spirit Dragon;
The shelled dwell in marshes, ruled by the Sacred Tortoise;
All streams converge, ruled by the River and Sea.

These nine rulers
Do not claim dominion over things,
Yet things return to them.
Without laws or measures,
Yet things govern themselves.
Without benevolence or righteousness,
Yet things attach to them.
Without exerting wisdom or strength,
Yet things fear them.

Why is this so?
They embody the Dao, harmonize with nature,
Do nothing to things,
Yet things transform under their influence.

Thus, the River and Sea’s rule
Is not through accumulating virtue or deeds,
Nor through flowing spirit and grace,
Nor through bestowing favors to win allegiance;
Nor through upholding rites and propriety,
Nor through eloquence and cunning,
Nor through displaying wisdom and skill,
Nor through extraordinary actions or schemes,
Nor through wielding might and terror,
Nor through exhausting life’s energies to control them—
But in stillness and humility, waiting below,
In non-action and non-seeking,
All streams come naturally.

The streams do not hear of the Sea’s beauty,
Yet they are transformed by its virtue and return in reverence.
Nor does the Sea bind or teach them,
Nor set paths or mark directions—
Yet water, piercing metal, shattering stone,
Boring cliffs, toppling mountains,
Rushing over hills,
Hastens to the Sea without return—
Form and nature align,
Things act naturally.

From this, it is seen:
Great is the Way of humility and self-effacement,
Harm cannot injure it,
Wisdom cannot seize it,
Strong without battle,
Mighty without coercion,
Silent and doing nothing,
Yet trading with all things.

Valleys are low but not east or west,
Thus they reach their end without exhaustion.
Rivers and seas dwell below but are not vast,
Thus they rule without ceasing.
Rivers and seas rule all valleys
Because they excel in humility—
Truly so.

Thus, the enlightened king and sage,
Desiring to honor the people,
Follows nature’s essence,
Nurtures virtue’s grace,
Speaks in humble words, bows with respect,
Associates with the common folk,
Views himself as the earth,
Serves the people as Heaven,
Illuminated in solitary knowing yet unfathomable,
Outstanding in singular ability yet untraceable,
Deep in insight, broad in reach, yet impassable,
Clear and bright, flowing yet unobstructed—
Not to dominate things,
But to follow, placing the people first.

The Sage, though possessing such abilities,
Does not place himself above others—
For he speaks humbly, does not lead—
For he follows with his own person.
Thus, the people willingly follow without tiring.

Therefore, the people tread upon him as upon the earth,
Trust him as though he were divine.
Ever above them, his land aligns with Heaven’s decree.

If he seeks to lead, yet dwells in obscurity,
Holds the highest honor, yet lives long and unharmed,
Finds joy in carefree existence—
Then he abandons ambition, forsakes self,
To ensure the people’s ease.
He labors while the people rest,
Retreats while the people advance,
Stands above while the people thrive,
Leads while the people find security.

Because the people thrive, they revere him without burden,
Because the people are secure, they follow him without distress.
The reason they revere him without burden
Is that he benefits them—
Thus, though he stands before them, they suffer no harm.

Therefore, the world pushes him upward,
Draws him forward,
Delights in him without weariness,
Takes joy in him without satiety.

Thus, the Sage’s rule is not one of seeking the people—
The people seek him.
Not one of benefiting the people—
The people benefit from him.
Not one of honoring the people—
The people honor him.
Not one of leading the people—
The people lead through him.

Thus, he can exhaust hardship, adapt to change,
Revive decay, rectify chaos,
Achieving supreme peace,
Above aligning with virtue and power,
Below encompassing the divine and mysterious,
Descending into the boundless,
Embracing the unfathomable,
Galloping through Heaven and Earth,
Ranging across yin and yang.

Why is this so?
Because he abandons the mind’s designs and follows with his body.

Thus, the virtue of non-contention,
Relies on the people’s strength,
Aligns with the Dao’s transformations,
Extends with the divine’s extremes—
Only those who abandon knowledge
Can follow its principles.

How could anyone contend with such a one?
Therefore, royal affairs proceed naturally,
Cannot be arbitrarily initiated,
Are attained through complete vitality,
Sustained by proper order,
Sage wisdom is innate,
Governance follows the Way.

To lose the order is to fail in royal affairs,
To abandon the Way is to misalign nature’s course.
Thus, the Sage trusts the Dao, not the self,
Follows the Dao, not the heart,
Acts not for self, but for others,
Does not claim the world—
Thus, the world contends to serve him.

Truly, these words are sincere.
The treasure of a nation,
Ten thousand emperors and kings cannot enact—
Only those who grasp even a fraction
Become the sovereign of all things.

The origin without end,
Returning to exhaustion and constraint.
To harmonize with its subtlety,
Without incurring self-harm.

This is called *clinging to constancy*,
Boundless and eternal.

The world calls me *manifest*—
The world calls me *with* and names me—
Greatly resembling the unworthy,
Formless as a shadow.

For only the great virtue is resplendent,
Thus resembling the unworthy—
Returning to the nature of illness.
If resembling, it is honored by the multitude,
Yet its end is small—
It defies the natural order.

I possess three treasures—
The Dao’s application.
Hold them and preserve them—
The heart’s origin.

First: *compassion*—
The initial injury.
Second: *frugality*—
Moderating oneself.
Third: *not daring to lead the world*—
Trusting what comes after.

Compassion thus generates courage—
The weak become strong.
Frugality thus expands—
Modesty yields growth.
Not daring to lead the world,
Trusting nature’s course,
Thus one can perfect the vessel—
The later born surpass the first.

Now, if one abandons compassion for rigidity,
Frugality for ostentation,
And "trusting what comes after" for leading,
Then death ensues—
The great fate is lost.

Compassion in war brings victory—
The people follow sincerely.
In defense, it secures—
The people’s loyalty is steadfast.
When Heaven intends to save,
It shields from calamity—
Enveloping life within.

The skilled warrior does not rely on force,
The skilled fighter does not rage,
The skilled victor does not retaliate,
The skilled ruler wins hearts—
Thus, their words and deeds are revered below.

This is called the virtue of *non-contention*—
Blessings spontaneously arise.

Using others’ strength to achieve their deeds—
This is called *aligning with Heaven*,
Matching Heaven’s course.
The ancient ultimate sages exhausted the Dao.

**Reflections:**
Things may share similarity yet differ,
Or differ yet share similarity,
Or appear false yet be true,
Or true yet be false—
Thus the sage knows no woe,
While the multitude suffers and grieves.

The sun and moon rise and set—
Different yet equally bright.
The wise and foolish share form,
Yet their hearts differ.
The sage harmonizes with the world without distress,
While the petty man seeks distinction—
Thus, he bears sorrow.

Hence it is said:
There is similarity within difference,
Truth within falsehood.

How does one demonstrate this?
Zhuangzi said:
The rising and setting of the sun and moon—
Their brightness is the same.
Human life and death—
Their form is identical.
The seasons’ divisions—
Their benefits are equal.
The sage and the commoner—
Their appearance is alike.
The open-minded and the closed—
Their deeds are the same.
The Daoist and the infant—
Their efficacy is identical.

All these things,
Though internally varied,
Externally align—
None but the sage can clarify them.

Thus, the world harbors suspicions,
Dazzled by false appearances,
Entangled in conflicting judgments—
Hence the sage appears unworthy.

The world calls me greatly unworthy.
Why so?
Those who grasp the Dao—
Externally absent, internally present,
Study to transform their nature,
Act to govern themselves,
Substantial yet appearing empty,
Mysterious and indistinct,
As if without purpose,
Speech sparse, demeanor humble,
Appearance crude and vulgar,
Yet their essence is boundless,
Their nature transcendent,
Quiet and withdrawn,
As if without end,
Avoiding renown, escaping influence,
Resting with the divine in rise and fall,
Embodying the Dao, walking in harmony,
All things in accord,
Not conforming to the times,
Friendly with Heaven and Earth.

The multitude, however, feigns and deceives,
Considers straightforwardness as ugliness,
Suffers from misdirection,
Calls it illness.

Only the great thus appears unworthy—
Hence the world deems it sickness.

The petty man is otherwise:
Wide learning, many knowledges,
Using wisdom to gain fame,
Unusual conduct, conspicuous deeds,
Creating doubt about virtue,
Deceiving the world, ordering customs,
Adorning speech, embellishing appearance,
Humbling himself to curry favor,
Anticipating intentions to win hearts,
Forming factions, harboring thieves.
Laboring little yet receiving heavy rewards,
Achieving few deeds yet holding high rank,
While the nation grows poor and his family rich,
The ruler weakens while he grows powerful.
Wielding power to create change,
Seizing advantage in prosperity,
His name and influence resound,
Shaking distant lands,
Arrogant, extravagant, violent,
Unrestrained, unchecked,
Compliant when favored, rebellious when opposed,
Actions costing lives, inaction breeding corruption,
Opening his mouth to harm the people.

This is what the world honors,
What society calls *resemblance*.

If so, then:
The Dao departs,
The divine withdraws,
Heaven and Earth abhor,
Yin and Yang reject,
Kindred reject him,
The people do not follow,
His actions meet no end,
His stillness yields no gain,
Life brings harm,
Death brings blessing—
The multitude, misled, leads others astray,
Their gains are meager,
Their days prolonged.

Thus, the petty man’s *resemblance* endures in triviality.

But those who grasp the Dao are otherwise:
Their body is empty, yet compassion accumulates,
They regard all things as themselves.

First: *compassion*.
Restraining form, moderating appearance,
Returning to subtlety.

Second: *frugality*.
Silent and withdrawn, trusting what comes after,
Not leading the world,
Harmonizing with nature’s course.

Third: *not daring to lead the world*.

Their actions and stillness contrast with the multitude,
Their comings and goings pass through different gates,
They do not use courage to be courageous—
Thus, they do not rage, yet the world fears them.
*Compassion thus generates courage.*

They do not use expansiveness to expand—
Thus, they do not bestow, yet the world follows them.
*Frugality thus expands.*

They do not lead—
Thus, without speaking, the world reveres them.
*Not daring to lead the world, thus perfecting the vessel.*

Hence, compassion transforms into courage,
Frugality into expansiveness,
"Trusting what comes after" into leadership—
Contrary to harmony, opposing the divine,
Defying nature’s course,
Blessings distance themselves,
Calamities draw near,
The great fate is severed,
Divine essence disperses,
Heaven and Earth cannot sustain,
The Dao and virtue cannot endure,
At death, unaware,
Blaming fate, reproaching Heaven.

Not because fate is meager,
Nor because men are malevolent—
But because they revel in arrogance,
Discard the Dao, and rely on force.

This is called:
Abandoning compassion for rigidity,
Frugality for ostentation,
"Trusting what comes after" for leadership—
Thus, death ensues.

For compassion as conduct—
Most harmonious and true,
In action, it mobilizes strength,
In stillness, it aligns with Heaven’s will,
Humbling the weak, ruling all things,
Rectifying the world, saving the people,
Harmonizing Heaven and humanity,
In war, undefeatable,
In defense, unassailable.

The benevolent one—
When waging war, triumphs;
When defending, stands firm—
This is its nature.

Why so?
Heaven and Earth arise together,
Transformations endless, without bound.

On the day of battle,
The Earth stirs beneath,
The Heavens tremble above,
Heaven sends omens of strife,
Earth unleashes winds of chaos,
Ghosts and spirits manifest,
To foes, misfortune comes;
It aids my generals and ministers,
It aids my myriad people,
Yet enrages my soldiers,
Extending to all living things.

Oxen lose their threshing,
Horses lose their grain,
Birds falter in flight,
Beasts stumble on land,
Tigers and rhinos may be fought,
Snatching birds may be led—
Foes in terror, crouching beneath ambush.

Heaven’s intent to save me,
Is through benevolence as my shield.

Thus, the worthy counselor to a victorious general
Does not rely on strength or grandeur,
Nor on rigidity or harshness,
But yields to softness and weakness, fearing force#9,
And the enemy then perishes.

The skilled soldier does not wield force.
In battle, he harms only outward intent,
Cherishing peace and harmony,
Declining small matters to trust in what follows,
In action and stillness, conforming to Heaven’s will,
Not stirring wrath to provoke anger,
Nor using force to raise armies.
The skilled warrior does not rage.

To overcome the enemy,
He always acts contrary to expectation,
Plans and movements unseen,
To capture the enemy’s resolve,
Actions spring from their own resources,
Transformations arise from their own deeds—
Reverse and overturn them,
To handle their unexpected moves.

Thus, when they act deceitfully, I stand upright,
When they speak, I listen,
When they grow angry, I rejoice,
When they move, I remain still,
Opening what they seek to exploit,
To preserve their lives.

The skilled victor does not contend.

In employing men,
I speak to them with humility,
Show them their benefits,
Explain to them with sincerity,
And let them come of their own accord.
The skilled leader serves below.

Thus, without contention or demand,
I gain the people’s intent,
I follow the people’s hearts,
Holding their key principles and prohibitions.
This is the virtue of non-contention.

When others scatter, I remain united,
When foes do not wish to perish, yet cannot,
When I do not wish to persist, yet cannot—
This is the power of employing men.

At such times,
The Dao is without form,
Virtue takes root,
Divine clarity serves as the standard,
Great harmony as the measure,
Pure and impure as my domain,
All things as my children,
The Three Lights as my aides,
The Four Seasons as my assistants,
Stillness as the origin of all things,
Movement as the beginning of transformation—
All beings entrust themselves to it,
Without fear of blame or retribution,
My virtue aligns with Heaven’s,
Enduring forever without peril.

From ancient to modern,
The Way of the sage and the wise,
In transformations from beginning to end,
From Heaven to kingship,
All derive from this.

This is called:
The ancient peak of matching Heaven.

**Dao De Zheng Jing (The True Classic of the Dao and Virtue) – Volume 11, *Zhi Gui* (Guidance) completed.**
**Volume 12, *Shu Jun Yan Zhen Zi Zhu* (Commentary by the Master of the Valley Spirit) on the *Dao De Zheng Jing* – compiled by Yan Zhen of Shu Commandery, styled Jun Ping.**

「」—This is a note of textual doubt and emendation.

#5 **Function and Fame**: Originally 『坊名』 (*Fāng Míng*), corrected according to the Yan Lan text.

#6 **Rest in Security**: Doubtful, possibly originally 『穴处』 (*Xuè Chù*).

#7 **Trickling Flow**: Originally 『涓须』 (*juān xū*), corrected according to the Yan Lan text.

#8 **These Few Things**: Originally 『比』 (*bǐ*), corrected according to the Secret Edition.

#9 **Not Firm nor Strong, Gentle and Weak Fear the Martial**: Originally 『不坚不柔，刚弱畏武』 (*Bù jiān bù róu, gāng ruò wèi wǔ*), corrected according to the Secret Edition.

**Volume 12 of the *Dao De Zheng Jing* (The True Classic of the Dao and Virtue) – *Zhi Gui* (Guidance) completed.**

**Commentary by the Master of the Valley Spirit on the *Dao De Zheng Jing* – compiled by Yan Zhen of Shu Commandery, styled Jun Ping.**

---

**Chapter on the Use of Arms**

The use of arms has a saying: *Chen Ji Gang* (陈纪纲) teaches it.

I do not dare to be the aggressor,
Nor to provoke the first strike—
Yet I follow as the guest,
And let the people act.

I do not dare to advance an inch,
Nor to retreat a foot in despair—
Yet I retreat a foot,
And hope they will return.

This is called:
**Action without action, countering with reverse.**
**Repelling without arms, yet causing no harm.**
**Seizing without weapons, foes find no response.**
**Yet when facing the enemy, there is none before me.**

**The greatest calamity is to slight the enemy and rely on oneself alone.**
**To slight the enemy risks the loss of my treasure—**
**The natural order of things.**

**Guidance:**

The Dao is ever-present, yet its presence does not act;
The Way is ever-true, yet its truth does not force.
Harmonize with nature, yield to weakness,
Yet embrace the great power within.
Nurture all beings, none can defy—
No form, yet the teacher of all things.
Who attains it finds security,
Who loses it faces peril.
Heaven and Earth embody it,
And endure without decay.

**How may one emulate it?**

Zhuangzi said:
**If there is no yin without yang, all things would not flourish;**
**If there is no yang without yin, all things would not complete.**

From this perspective,
**Power without virtue cannot govern the people;**
**Virtue without power risks the collapse of ancestral shrines.**
**Lacking both virtue and power is to invite disaster—**
**When fate shifts and changes, the ruler dies,
And the nation perishes.**

Thus, the ruler is the **vital core of the state**,
And the military is the **manifest power of the nation**.

Between Heaven and Earth,
All nations rise and fall,
Great or small, wise or foolish—
All wish to be sovereign.
The wise submit when their wisdom is exhausted,
The strong yield when their strength is spent.
They do not have excess power,
Yet kneel willingly as subjects.

Hence, the enlightened kings and sage rulers,
**Follow the Dao and emulate Heaven**,
**Prioritize tranquility and purity**,
**Maintain harmony and compliance**,
**Adapt to circumstances as their foundation**,
**Uphold sincerity and trust as their essence**,
**Align name and reality as their standard**,
**Employ rewards and punishments as their framework**,
**Balance virtue on the left and power on the right**,
**To counter misfortune**—
**The world looks to them for order,**
**None dare to harm or defy them.**
Thus, **the nation is preserved and the people are whole**.

**Virtue and power, like Heaven and Earth, yin and yang,**
**Are the martial and civil, surface and depth—**
**Balanced and harmonious,**
**Hidden in the mysterious realm,**
**Only employed when absolutely necessary.**

**Thus, when a ruler loses the Dao,**
**Loves war and harms himself,**
**Neglects proper governance,**
**And indulges in corruption,**
**He grows arrogant and extravagant,**
**Disregards laws and weakens discipline,**
**Adorns palaces and lavishes on horses,**
**Builds lofty towers and vast enclosures,**
**Pursues music and beauty—**
**He slaughters loyal advisors,**
**Honors favorites and beauties,**
**Disrespects ancestral shrines,**
**Insults elders,**
**Oppresses with cruelty and violence,**
**Leaves widows and orphans to perish,**
**Defies Heaven and Earth,**
**Violates the order of yin and yang,**
**Disfigures virtue and power,**
**Shatters divine clarity,**
**Dissolves harmonious qi into filth,**
**And disrupts the natural flow of transformation—**
**Then winter thunders and summer frosts arise,**
**And all things wither and die.**

**With armies clashing and strategies unraveling,**
**Plots bring only misfortune—**
**Great nations tremble in fear,**
**Small nations flee in terror,**
**The elderly and weak scatter,**
**Weeping as they depart—**
**The world seethes with resentment,**
**All people long for peace,**
**Yet rise in rebellion,**
**And I am left to follow their lead.**

**Why is this so?**

It is because **their ancestors**,
**Each possessed divine virtue and wisdom,**
**Communed with Heaven and Earth,**
**And labored for the benefit of all people.**
Thus, **they were granted the Mandate of Heaven,**
**Received the land and noble lineage,**
**Elevated as rulers,**
**Their glory shining upon their forebears,**
**Their deeds passed down to future generations.**

**This is the heart of Heaven and Earth,**
**The enduring merit of ten thousand years.**
Yet **those who inherit this legacy**,
**Do not strive to humble themselves,**
**Refine their essence,**
**Or cultivate the Dao and virtue—**
**Instead, they ignore small virtues and embrace small vices,**
**Growing worse day by day, month by month.**
**Ancestral shrines crumble,**
**The nation becomes a ruin,**
**Clans scatter and disperse,**
**The young have no refuge—**
**Ghosts and spirits, orphaned souls,**
**Find no resting place.**

**Alas! How can such a nation not decline?**

Thus, I instruct **my brave and bold warriors,**
And speak to **my soldiers.**
**The use of arms has a saying.**

**Unarm the soldiers and release the weapons,**
**Yet let them rest and recover.**
**Exhausted by battle, their spirits shift—**
**They may yet repent and change,**
**Transform their appearance and ease their burdens.**

**I do not dare to be the aggressor,**
**Yet I follow as the guest.**
**To march forward without return is to become a thief to the world—**
**The people suffer to the extreme,**
**Their wealth exhausted, strength spent,**
**And woes grow daily.**
**Heaven responds in kind,**
**Disaster gathers upon the land—**
**Though I wish to spare them,**
**Destruction comes naturally.**
**Heaven and people align in heart,**
**I cannot overcome them.**

**I do not dare to advance an inch,**
**Nor retreat a foot in despair—**
**Yet I retreat a foot,**
**And hope they will return.**

Thus, **the mission is accomplished,**
**Yet the spirit is weary;**
**The body moves forward,**
**Yet the resolve lingers behind.**
**The form opposes my will,**
**The deed defies my purpose.**
**Action without action.**

**Pity those sage ancestors,**
**And lament the suffering of the people—**
**They had no fault before Heaven,**
**Yet why did they meet such ruin?**

Though it seems sudden,
**The lives of the people depend on war—**
**When orders are given with sincerity and trust,**
**They must be obeyed.**
**Those who submit first are rewarded,**
**Those who submit late are harmed,**
**The small nations are benefited,**
**The great nations thrive—**
**Those who surrender their lands are granted titles,**
**Those who yield their cities are given fiefs.**
**If cities and lands do not surrender,**
**Do not despair if they are not yet conquered.**

**The guilty are pardoned,**
**The capable are appointed,**
**The elderly and weak are provided for,**
**The dead are given proper burial.**
**Repelling without arms shows no harm.**

**Virtue and benevolence spread far and wide,**
**Love and harmony flow among the people,**
**Kindness and grace bind them together—**
**All hearts are uplifted,**
**And without a word, their spirits align with mine.**
**Seizing without weapons:**

**If my three armies share my resolve,**
**The treacherous become my messengers,**
**The thieves become my laborers—**
**They teach me their strategies,**
**And reveal their terrain.**
**Using their own resources,**
**My clever schemes succeed.**

**Laws are not harsh,**
**Dangerous obstacles are left unguarded—**
**Heaven and people cooperate,**
**Victory comes like the divine.**
**No enemy remains before me,**
**No strategy is lost—**
**Though the foe be many,**
**They provoke me,**
**And when the two armies stand apart,**
**They face none before them.**

**Yet there is no enemy:**

**Though war is fully prepared,**
**The enemy cannot be changed.**
**To change the enemy breeds deceit,**
**And when defeat comes, I lose advantage—**
**Fortune departs, calamity arrives,**
**And I am despised by Heaven.**
**The greatest calamity is to insult the enemy.**
Thus, **even if I gain Heaven’s favor**
**And the aid of the people,**
**I still hold them with concern,**
**And grasp them with caution—**
**Exiting peril, seizing opportunity,**
**My encampments are fortified,**
**My resting places secure,**
**I rely on water and grass,**
**Fill gaps and block flaws,**
**And maintain distance from the enemy.**

「If I insult the enemy, I shall perish—
Thus, I must act as I do.

The Dao’s flow is formless,
Its stratagems emerge unseen,
Its victories unfold endlessly,
Its battles leave no trace.
Profound and subtle, its transformations know no beginning,
Yet it remains close to me—
I do not grasp its workings,
Nor do they comprehend my power.
How, then, can they benefit me?

Therefore, the household dwells in ease and finds joy,
The disciple rejoices and takes delight.
When people harbor utmost sincerity,
They treat me as their own child—
Leaving home and crossing borders,
They gather like crowds at a marketplace.
They toil in farming and weaving with vigor,
Rest at dusk and rise at dawn,
Their movements follow disciplined formation,
No one dares act alone,
Supplying teacher and pupil
As if honoring father and mother.

Weighing advantage to claim victory,
The enemy rises not.
No battle fought, yet all under Heaven submit,
No assault launched, yet the feudal lords bow.
Heaven and Earth shelter and nurture all,
Sun and Moon illuminate and reveal all—
Majestic and boundless,
Each content in its own domain.

Why so?—
Compassion and mercy set things in motion,
Aligning with Heaven’s intent,
Not daring to act against its will.
When armies face each other,
The merciful prevail.

**My words are easy to understand—**
**Yet none can know the unknowable.**
**My actions are simple to follow—**
**Yet none can emulate the unemulated.**

Yet my words have a lineage,
My deeds a sovereign principle—
Unspoken yet inherent,
