Natural yet unforced.
Only those who fail to recognize me
And differ from my ways
Do not comprehend me,
Do not affirm my truth.
Few know me—
Common folk say, *"Then you are noble, you alone are true."*

Thus, the Sage—
The wise ruler—
Clad in coarse garments, humble in body,
Yet treasures virtue within, rich in spirit.
**Pointing to the source:**
What is formless and soundless yet guides all things naturally?
The **Dao/Way** and the **Divine**#5.
What is formed and audible yet guides all things naturally?
Earth and Heaven.
The **Divine’s** transformations surge and flow,
Heaven and Earth speak in silence.
The surge transforms all without exception,
The silence reveals all without fail.
**Divine qi**#6 and vitality pass on,
Responding to each other’s impulses—
Returning to boundless emptiness,
Exquisitely subtle and wondrous,
They rest in nature.

They lead all things without end.

Thus, **spoken words are but tools of expression—**
**Action is the ruin of authenticity.**
**Non-action, no words—**
**This is the apex of achievement**
**And the key to enduring life.**

Therefore, the Sage speaks of what is unspeakable,
Acts without doing—
Words to silence words,
Actions to halt actions.
**The path of ceasing speech:**
Discard the heart and mind’s designs,
**The art of halting action:**
Abandon human cleverness and wisdom.
Become foolish and guileless,
Know nothing, desire nothing.
Without desire comes stillness,
Stillness brings emptiness,
Emptiness yields substance,
Substance births the **Divine**#7.
Movement returns to the **Great Primordial**#8,
Stillness to nature—
Preserve the self, safeguard the realm,
Wealth and honor fade not,
All creatures fulfill their aspirations,
And attain lasting life.

These are words easy to grasp,
Actions simple to follow—
Yet Heaven and Earth cannot fathom them,
Cannot emulate them.
Why so?

The rulers of this world delight in knowledge,
Strive to please their hearts,
See not the Great Dao,
Recognize not nature—
They take action as action,
Speak words as words,
Exhausting knowledge to spawn deeds,
Seeking comfort in their own inclinations,
Yet they deem this as the common way,
And suffer afflictions all their lives—
Their essence fades, their emotions shift,
They sink deep into confusion and stray far from truth,
Their vitality dulls, their spirit unsettles,
Their bodies appear solid yet their inner selves are hollow,
Forced silence breeds guilt,
Forced stillness invites calamity.

Thus, they look yet do not see,
They are told yet do not hear—
Not from spite, but because they believe otherwise.
They refuse loyal counsel,
Not from spite toward themselves,
But because they think no such principle exists,
And so they do not follow it.

The Sage’s words spring from nature,
Take root in the **Divine**#9—
Ever dwelling in their opposite#10,
Between silence and utterance.
Exquisitely subtle and wondrous,
They return to nature—
Bright yet as unseen,
Heard yet as unheard,
Permeating yet appearing blocked,
Reaching yet seeming confined.

Their deeds begin in nature,
Flow through the **Divine**—
Ever abiding in harmony,
Between action and inaction.
**Pure, still, yielding, weak—**
**Movement refined, actions delicate—**
**Simplicity, ease, retreat, and self-effacement**
**Return to non-action.**

This is the **source of words, the master of deeds.**

Emptiness unites with the **Dao/Way**,
Serenity dwells in life,
Timely harmony secures the realm,
Establishing teachings to nurture the people.
**Nourish with no desire—**
**Guide with nature—**
**Bestow with Heaven and Earth—**
**Grant with mountains and rivers—**
**Enrich with years and seasons—**
**Honor with existence—**
**Protect with no worry—**
**Ensure with no calamity—**
Without desire, nothing is lost,
Without joy, nothing is sustained.
People without a ruler,
A ruler without people—
Perish through knowledge and cleverness,
Rely on the **Dao/Way**, lean on Heaven—
All nations in harmony,
United under one sovereign.

This is the grandest of undertakings,
The most exalted of achievements—
Yet the world deems it otherwise.
The **Dao/Way** makes it so,
Logic knows no deception.
The wise are clouded by their own understanding,
Thus they refuse to believe.

The world cannot know me,
Not because my **Dao/Way** is insignificant,
Nor because my deeds are shallow,
Nor because the world delights in ruin and evil,
Rejoices in disaster and misfortune—
Heaven’s nature opposes mine,
Human desires diverge from my truth,
Wisdom ensnared by desires
Endures weakness all its life;
**Divine qi** does not affirm me,
Hearts and minds do not accept my words.

Thus, they cannot see my clarity,
Cannot hear my insight—
Deep words invite slander,
Great deeds invite reproach,
Unique vision is unused by the world,
Unheard wisdom shames the people.
Deeds that align with the **Divine** are not embraced by convention,
Achievements that match Heaven and Earth please not the masses.

Few know me.

**The ultimate discourse, the greatest words—**
They encompass all variations,
Grasp all directions,
Divide even the finest distinctions,
Illuminate the void beyond form and perception—
Boundless and thorough,
Yet return to **obscurity and ambiguity**#11.

This is what petty scholars cannot hear,
And narrow-minded folk cannot attain.»

The lofty wild goose soars all day,
Yet never knows what lies beyond the cosmos.

Those who craft laws and bind themselves to teachings,
Study for years, debate a lifetime,
Yet never grasp the vastness of **virtue/power**.

Moreover, there are things even the **Divine** cannot comprehend,
Things even Heaven and Earth cannot govern—
How much more so for those who cling to common doctrines,
Who fail to perceive the fundamental principles?

Thus, what the multitude despises as worthless
Is what those who attain the **Dao/Way** alone uphold.
Therefore, I am esteemed.

Hence, the Sage knows yet does not act,
Is capable yet does not assume responsibility,
Practices benevolence and righteousness yet does not proclaim them,
Is enlightened yet does not name it so,
Is steadfast yet does not flaunt it,
Is towering yet does not take pride—
Words cannot be heard,
Actions cannot be seen,
The heart vast as the sea,
The will boundless as the sky,
Abandoning artifices to establish the **Dao/Way**,
Diminishing superficial virtues to nurture true nature.

Silent and boundless, none can fathom,
Luminous yet unseen, none can discern its essence,
Crumbled yet enduring, alone it stands,
Thin without, thick within,
Humble in self, lowly in name,
Abandoning the crowd, departing from convention,
Taking the **Dao/Way** as constant.

Clad in coarse robes yet cradling jade within.
Knowing yet feigning ignorance—this is the chapter.

The unknown does not know—this is the Way of returning to the boundless.
The known knows not—this is the sickness of mistaking ignorance for wisdom.

Only the sickly cling to cleverness and profit,
Thus they are not plagued by harm nor enslaved by the crowd.

The Sage does not fret over non-existence,
For fretting over the **Dao/Way**’s unknown is the sickness itself—
Thus they are not troubled by calamity nor ensnared by comparison.

**Chapter on Returning:**
The teaching of **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way** is naturalness itself.
The verification of naturalness is resonance—
All things have form and sound,
Taking and discarding echo with resonance—
Not only the myriad creatures.

When form moves, it does not generate form but generates shadow;
When sound moves, it does not generate sound but generates echo.
The non-existent generates the existent without itself existing,
The covering generates reversal without itself being covered.

Thus, the **Dao/Way** governs through *wuwei* (non-action),
Yet the knowing are vexed by excessive affairs.
The infant thrives through ignorance,
The aged decline through overreaching.

From this, it is seen:
Folly is the shape of clever wisdom,
Clever wisdom is the shadow of folly.
*Wuwei* is the original sound of spontaneous action;
Spontaneous action is the echo of *wuwei*.
Clever wisdom is the net of chaos,
Action is the rope of ruin.

Folly, through *wuwei*, is like form and sound—
The root, thus able to generate clever wisdom.
Action, like shadow and echo—
The branch, thus bringing disorder and ruin.

Hence, the myriad things cannot be harmonized,
Heaven and Earth cannot be conformed to.
Harmonizing leads to the loss of harmony,
Conforming leads to the loss of conformity.
Only by not harmonizing can harmony be achieved,
Only by not conforming can conformity be attained.

Speaking of clever wisdom’s use,
Chasing traces spawns waves—
The principles of harmony and conformity
Are extinguished with time.

Thus, action is not permissible,
Non-action is permissible.
Hence, the secure do not know danger,
The chaotic do not know governance.

Like shadows following forms, there is no escape;
Seeking shadows without movement yields nothing.
Thus, knowing yet severing knowledge—
Not constrained by knowing.
Self-destruction is its nature,
Thus it is not constrained.

Not using knowing to know—
Also not constrained by knowing.
Though its use is lost,
It does not know knowing as use—
Thus both are unconstrained.

Their unconstrained ways differ,
Yet in not being constrained, they are one.
Both do not use knowing—thus one.

Yet severing knowledge is for oneself,
Using knowledge is for others—
Thus they differ.

Therefore, the Sage cultivates the nature of openness,
Roams in the realm of profound silence,
Dwells in the land of incapacity,
Entrusts the body of non-knowing—
Silent as empty space,
Fleeting as death,
The heart scheming for nothing,
The will governing nothing,
Yet clarity and insight move,
Illuminating the four seas,
Dulling the people’s ears and eyes,
Showing them emptiness—
Kitchens unformed, sounds and colors not aroused,
Knowledge does not arise, calamities cease.

Without speech, the world is governed;
Without action, the world is transformed.
The people bow without rebellion,
Look up without imitation,
Sincere and upright, each guards their true nature—
Dazed and vast, all fulfill their natural end.

The dead have no posthumous names,
The living have no titles—
Thus they succeed endlessly, numberless as the sands of time.

Through knowing ignorance, such benefits arise—
Thus called the highest.

The next level:
Emotion finds no delight,
Nature no joy,
The heart no ease,
The will no gain—
Hating ignorance of filial piety,
Sickened by ignorance of fraternal duty,
Neither dimming **virtue/power** nor recognizing righteousness,
No rites or ceremonies,
Guarding their innate purity—
Like deer or wild geese,
Neither in sorrow nor in chaos,
As blind, as deaf—
No palaces for rulers,
No cities for the people,
No laws for the land,
No shame in the world.

Through deficient knowing,
They perform actions like sickness.

Thus it is said:
The known knows not—this is the sickness of mistaking ignorance for wisdom.

The sickly are not sick,
Entrusting themselves to the **Dao/Way**,
Neither speaking nor acting,
Yet **virtue/power** and awe arise naturally.

Heaven and Earth in harmony,
Mingled and vast,
Like turbid waters settling,
Like a dignified guest arriving—
All creatures return to simplicity,
Vanishing into stillness,
Moving without dismemberment,
Resting without fracture—
Rulers and people flourish together,
The world in quietude,
Perishing in trivial gains,
Yet enjoying great blessings.

Silent governance—
Plans cannot be plotted,
Measurements cannot be taken.
The Sage always seems sickly,
Thus not sick—
Able to align with the **Dao/Way**,
Beyond calculation or measure.

If differing from this,
Knowledge arises and deceit takes form.

How to demonstrate this?
When **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way** decay,
Purity vanishes,
Strange things proliferate,
Knowledge spreads,
Rites and norms emerge,
Rankings and measures are clear—
The myriad things have order,
A hundred affairs have codes.
Borders are drawn, cities built,
States divided into districts and villages.
Nations have loyal ministers,
Families have filial children,
Inner records, outer omissions,
Paired bodies as companions,
Taking from one to give to another,
Striving for mutual enrichment—
The strong grow mighty, the weak perish.
Sacrifices to Heaven, Earth, great mountains and rivers,
Enfeoffments at Tai Mountain,
Pilgrimages to Liangfu—
Gradual traditions, some true, some false.
Thousands of lawsuits decided,
Branded criminals fill the roads,
Subjects kill their rulers,
Sons kill their fathers,
Nations fall, families break—
Numberless.
The world reaps the benefits of knowledge,
Yet suffers the guilt of death.

The knowing of ignorance,
Yet forcing knowledge upon ignorance—
This is the true knowing.

From this, it is clear:
Severing knowledge is blessing,
Loving knowledge is ruin.

Thus, even a **virtue/power**-filled ruler,
Changing resolve and heart,
Generating myriad affairs to teach the people,
Praying and sacrificing for great governance—
Naturalness will not comply.

If a **virtue/power**-filled ruler temporarily abandons the **Dao/Way** and indulges desire,
Then blessing does not become blessing—
This is the nature of rites.

If a fallen ruler discards cleverness and desire,
Severs all to halt affairs,
Cultivates the **Dao/Way** and practices **virtue/power**,
Abandons heart and intent, even seeking death—
Their form will not obey.

Now the fallen ruler temporarily turns his heart toward the **Dao/Way**,
Then ruin does not become ruin—
Thus, the form does not obey.

Therefore, the rush and retreat, the abandoning and taking up,
Are the **Dao/Way** of the Sage-King’s form and sound.

Life and death, prosperity and ruin,
Are the echoes of rush and retreat.

The Sage, thus, can move in harmony with Heaven,
And rest in unity with the **Dao/Way**,
Preserving his body while governing the realm,
Soaring beyond all doing,
At ease within the void of affairs,
Drawing blessing from the subtlest threads,
And spreading it beyond the four seas.

The Sage embodies the knowing of ignorance,
Though it seems like sickness, yet he does all things,
Never missing the slightest hint—
Thus, he blocks the ruin of petty doing,
And fulfills the greatness of non-doing.
Hence, it is said: *Take blessing from the subtlest threads,*
And spread it beyond the four seas.

Plug the eyes of the sighted,
Seal the mouths of the knowing,
Stop the ears of the hearing,
Sever the hands of the clever—
Follow the rhythms of time,
Bow and rise with the harmony.

Without doing, yet all is accomplished;
Without teaching, yet the people are governed.
Grace extends to fleeing beasts,
Kindness reaches flying birds—
For he discards hearing and wisdom,
Abandons doing and trusts the **Dao/Way**.

Because he treats sickness as sickness,
Thus he does not fall ill.
If he did not treat sickness as sickness,
Then he would not be correct.

Thus, following emotion and desire,
Exhausting the depths of heart and intent,
Moving to guide Heaven and Earth,
Resting to nurture all things,
Divine spirit within himself,
Unseen and unburned—
Without descending from his seat,
All nations prepare themselves.
Even if he wished not to perish,
Nature itself would not allow it.

If one turns away from the **Dao/Way** and follows desire,
Though he hopes to survive, he must fail—
For the strong and violent do not meet their end naturally;
This is the inevitable order.

Hence, it is said: *Nature itself would not allow it.*
*Allow* means *halt*.

The people do not fear great authority—
If the people do not fear authority, they easily do wrong—
Then great authority arrives, detached from its form.

Do not cling to what you possess, for it is low and weak;
Do not exhaust what you are given, for it is constrained and perilous.

Only those who do not weary of sweetness’s joy
Will not weary of enduring hardship.

Thus, the Sage says to the ruler:
*"Know yourself, but do not reveal yourself—*
*Then you are not called virtuous.*
*Love yourself, but do not exalt yourself above the people—*
*Then you take from that and seek this within yourself."*

**Key Principle:**
The essence of **Dao** and **virtue/power**,
The divine affairs of the numinous,
The great harmony of the heart,
The intent of Heaven and Earth—
No calamity is greater than ruin,
No blessing is greater than survival.
This is not only the **Dao/Way** of Heaven,
But also of all things and beings.

Thus, the **Dao/Way** of preserving life
Is nothing more urgent than nurturing the spirit;
The essence of nurturing the spirit
Is nothing greater than simplicity.

Always embodying reverence and dread,
Trembling and quaking—
Losing the spirit’s guardianship
Stems from indulgence;
Losing the spirit’s measure
Lies in self-assertion.

Thus, the highest reverence is for the **Dao/Way**,
Next for Heaven,
Then for Earth,
Then for humanity,
Then for oneself.

Prosperity and decline, fortune and ruin—
All arise from oneself.
Do not fear what is subtle,
But you must fear what is manifest.

Do not ponder small faults,
Or they will become great offenses.
Suffering great calamity and deep ruin,
Until annihilation—
Reverence and dread must begin at the origin,
Ascending to the formless and soaring.

Guard and be cautious before signs appear,
Then the **Dao/Way** shines greatly.
In action, you attain what you desire;
In stillness, you attain what is tranquil.
Blessing and fortune are profound and subtle,
Lost in boundlessness.

Guard and be cautious before signs appear,
Fear what has not yet been heard—
Hence, it is said: *Reverence and dread at the origin*
Leads to profound blessing and fortune.

Straight speech seems contrary,
Yet it is clear as if in darkness,
Vast and deep, beyond hearing.

Waiting for fate is within yourself—
What need is there of Heaven?
Greatest blessing turns to calamity,
Greatest fortune seems like misfortune.
When the world is drunk and sated,
None can perceive it.

Thus, the common folk see only what is near,
Hear only what is shallow,
And do not recognize the **Dao/Way** of the profound and mysterious.
They are blocked by the subtle and constant,
Sealed from the divine logic,
Examining the profit of a hair’s breadth,
Yet failing to see the ruin of mountains.
They indulge emotion and behavior,
Fearless and uninhibited.

Speech follows what is so,
Action follows what is good,
Using strength to take what is possible,
The heart complying with delusion—
Proud, extravagant, arrogant, and contentious,
Self-assertive and self-diminishing.

Loyalty and trust are what they love,
Yet they deceive even their parents,
Plunder Heaven and Earth,
Disrespect their sovereign,
Pursuing emotion and desire,
Thus defying the **Dao/Way** of Heaven.

Hence, they devise clever schemes and false names
To replace their true selves,
Bestow favors and spread kindness
Yet invite great ruin,
Flee blessings beyond Heaven,
Chase calamities across the seas—
Seeking **virtue/power** and blessing,
Yet cannot obtain them;
Calamity and disaster pursue,
With no escape.

Because they do not fear great authority,
Great authority arrives.
When great authority arrives,
Only then do they begin to do good.

At this point,
**Dao** and **virtue/power** cannot save,
Heaven and Earth cannot resolve—
This is not Heaven’s fault.
They delight in height and excess,
Bear great authority and rely on power,
Lacking dread and reverence,
Seeking nothing within themselves.

Thus, those who dread for their own bodies
Need not fear others;
Those who fear within themselves
Need not be controlled by others;
Those who comply with the small
Need not fear the great;
Those who warn of the near
Need not regret the distant.

If one can self-admonish and self-strengthen,
Being cautious each day,
Then ruin will not come from afar,
And blessing will be near.

Thus, one does not despise his station,
Yet resides in it with diligence;
Does not slight his position,
Yet enriches his conduct with propriety;
Does not weary of lowliness,
Yet transforms his great beginnings;
Does not tire of hardship and peril,
Yet severs what leads to it—
Finding joy in adversity as in success,
Accepting death as peacefully as longevity.
Even if he wished to linger,
He could not remain long.

Not shunning the bitterness of his station,
Though low and small, he refines it further,
Thus transforming its great essence—
If he finds sweetness in it without wearying,
Then he will not remain in it long.

How is this made clear?
Zhuangzi said:
*The **Dao/Way** of Heaven and Earth*
*Begins and yet has an end,*
*Ends and yet returns to beginning.*

When yang-qi rests as the hidden dragon,
It can melt metal;
When yin-qi is at ease with frost,
It can freeze ice;
When wood thrives in the autumn hair’s breadth,
It can grow to a hundred fathoms;
When water delights in the trickling stream,
It can become the sea;
When birds take joy in the egg’s warmth,
They can soar high;
When beasts prepare in the womb’s embrace,
They can run far.

「Therefore, the sage’s wisdom is boundless and vast,
He can align with Heaven’s **Dao/Way**,
Transforming and moving with its shifts,
Piercing through the great constancy—
Yet still he accumulates **virtue/power** in depth,
Releases his mind’s intentions,
Conceals his keen insight,
Fears the unseen and the formless,
Hides his traces and vanishes his appearance,
Retreats to emptiness and returns to **玄冥** (Xuanming, the mysterious and dark),
Though his body weighs as much as Heaven and Earth, he does not exalt himself,
Though his **virtue/power** surpasses yin and yang, he does not display it.
Thus the sage knows himself yet does not reveal himself.

He entrusts himself to the humble and few,
Placing himself last among the myriad people,
His food lacks flavor, his garments lack adornment,
His actions are plain yet he dares not indulge his desires.
He cherishes himself yet does not esteem himself.

He upholds the **Dao/Way** and practices **virtue/power**,
Rising and falling with all things,
Nurturing the people as a father,
Meeting the masses like a sovereign,
His **virtue/power** ascending to Heaven,
His achievements credited to others,
The world renounces him in favor of others,
His benevolence deep and enduring—
Thus calamity cannot harm him while misfortune cannot plague him#6,
Fortune cannot escape him while **virtue/power** does not vanish.

Not the **Dao/Way** of harmony with Heaven and Earth,
Nor the **Dao/Way** of partiality toward the world—
He guards against the slightest beginnings,
Maintains loyalty and sincerity in weakness,
Follows the **Dao/Way** and aligns with Heaven,
Resonates with all things in their natural course,
Fears calamity yet finds joy in it,
Embraces ease yet endures hardship,
Success and failure, life and death—
These he seeks within himself.
Abandoning arrogance and excess,
Taking humility and meekness instead.
**Courage and daring:**
If one dares in courage, he brings slaughter, defeat, and injury;
If one dares not in courage, he achieves vitality, joy, and longevity.
These two paths teach life and death,
Sometimes beneficial, sometimes harmful—
A matter of gain or loss, of fortune or woe.

What Heaven detests is easily named,
Yet who knows its reasons? None can explain.
The **Dao/Way** of Heaven is both yin and yang,
It does not contend yet naturally prevails—
Following the forms of things,
Responding without words to their sounds,
Summoning none yet all come of their own accord—
Things reveal themselves naturally.
It is plain yet masterfully strategizes—
Things disclose their own dispositions.
The Heavenly net is vast and boundless,
Fine as though nonexistent yet nothing escapes—
Wide yet nothing is lost, retaining what should not be abandoned#7.

**The principle of alignment:**
The **Dao/Way** of Heaven and Earth,
The logic of life and death,
Has neither departure nor approach,
Neither taking nor giving,
Neither action nor inaction—
Yet all things arise naturally.
Straight as a plumb line,
Smooth as water,
Responding to circumstances and emulating patterns,
Rising with all things in harmony,
Gaining and losing, taking and discarding,
Completing tasks from beginning to end,
Deep or shallow, light or heavy—
All things take their own measure,
Diverse forms and kinds,
Each returning to its natural state#8.
Life is the root of death,
Death is the origin of life—
These two, when contrasted,
Reveal fortune and misfortune.

Thus, he who knows life yet not death
Inverts Heaven’s order;
He who knows death yet not life
Defies Earth’s principle.
Hence, joy and anger have their limits,
Life and death their measures—
Receiving Heaven’s punishment,
Enduring Earth’s retribution.
When anger is due yet not expressed,
His sons become wolves and his brothers oxen#9;
When battle is called yet not fought,
His wives become enemy states and his concubines great bandits#10;
When death is decreed yet not carried out,
He invites Heaven’s wrath and is dominated by things;
When joy is owed yet not given,
He suffers Heaven’s disaster and meets Earth’s blame;
When life is granted yet not nurtured,
Rulers lose their kingdoms and commoners their lives.
Thus the noble man kills his people as he would his own body,
Gives life to others as he would to himself,
Holding fast to **virtue/power** and uprightness,
Acting only when necessity compels him.

To preserve life and ensure the nation’s peace,
Lies in the balance of life and death—
If life and death are governed rightly,
Heaven and Earth will protect him,
And the measure of joy and anger
Will bring all things to him.
Hence, he is firm yet resolute,
Strikes deep and with severity,
Repels evil and nourishes the good,
Dares yet knows restraint.

Skilled in defense and war,
Clear in opening and closing,
Long in resentment yet swift in retribution,
At ease in stillness yet joyful in action,
Benefiting the nation and the people,
Not avoiding the strong or mighty,
His威令 (威令, awe and authority) shakes the hundred barbarians,
His influence sways the borders beyond—
He attains half of what is good.
This is from daring in courage.

The meek and weak, fearing and respecting,
Dreading to harm others with words,
Reflecting deeply to grasp far-reaching thoughts,
Confronting justice yet punishing grudges,
Pursuing longevity and few conflicts,
Accumulating benevolence through broad and profound deeds.
He benefits without harming,
To make his goodness known;
He gives without taking,
To manifest his name;
He rewards without punishing,
To establish his grace;
He grants life without killing,
To fulfill his仁 (ren, compassion)—
He attains half of what is good.
This is from daring not to dare.

In all these two achievements—
Daring in courage and daring not to dare—
Their strategies diverge yet both strike the mark,
Their paths contradict yet both find their purpose—
One may turn a thousand chariots into captives,
Another may transform an ordinary man into a sovereign.
Thus, things may be nurtured for fortune or for calamity,
Or slain for fortune or for ruin.
These two principles are profound and subtle,
None can fully fathom them.
Unable to grasp them fully,
They do not perceive the mysterious and dark’s disposition,
Nor distinguish the divisions of calamity and fortune.
Only those who attain the **Dao/Way** comprehend the unseen before it occurs,
And can thus govern its beginnings—
Beyond the reach of ordinary measures.
Hence it is said that these two principles are profound and subtle,
None can fully explore them.

Thus, nurturing what Heaven detests,
Harms what Heaven protects;
Nurturing what Heaven protects,
Harms what Heaven detests.
One reversal, one inversion,
May yield **玄德** (Xuanming, mysterious virtue);
One inversion, one reversal,
May breed **玄贼** (Xuanming, mysterious ruin).
Serve Heaven and Earth as a father,
And your descendants will prosper.
Thus, if you nurture yet the future declines,
Life was meant to perish;
If you slay yet fortune arrives,
Death was meant to live;
If you slaughter yet blessings follow,
Killing was meant to bring fortune;
If you destroy yet misfortune comes,
Killing was meant to nurture life.
What Heaven detests, do not dare to preserve;
What Heaven protects, do not dare to slay;
What Heaven diminishes, do not dare to give;
What Heaven benefits, do not dare to take.
To give or not to give,
Only Heaven decides;
To slay or not to slay,
No mortal knows.
Thus, daring in courage often leads to harm and defeat,
Daring not to dare aligns with Heaven’s decree.»

「Thus, the daring that does not dare aligns with Heaven’s decree—
in action, it harmonizes with the **Dao** of Heaven,
in stillness, it reaches the **extreme** of Earth.
What Heaven’s heart detests, none can justify.
Who knows the reason for Heaven’s displeasure?

The **Dao** of Heaven and Earth:
one **yin** and one **yang**,
divides into the four seasons,
separates into the five **phases**,
flows into the ten thousand things,
refines into the three luminaries.
**Yang qi** governs **virtue/power**,
**yin qi** governs punishment,
encompassing all beings,
breathing life to distant lands,
mysterious and impartial,
upright and just.

It does not favor life for cleverness,
nor punish killing for skillfulness,
responding to all things,
never acting alone—
auspiciousness with auspiciousness,
calamity with calamity,
harming those who do evil,
rewarding those who do good.
Thus, without action, things arise;
without action, things perish.
Shadows mingle, echoes resonate—
without seeking, things are obtained;
without constraint, things conform;
without scrutiny, things reveal themselves;
without inquiry, things express their nature.
This is the **Dao** of Heaven.

Hence, without strife, one conquers all;
without words, one responds to all;
without summoning, all come.
Silent and vast,
planning all without effort—
this is called conquering without strife,
responding without words,
summoning without call,
and planning without speech.

The **Dao** is woven in **惚恍** (huǎnghuǎng, indistinct yet discernible),
yet its net is formless—
sprawling yet boundless,
far-reaching yet lingering,
penetrating without gaps,
adapting to all things.
Thus, it precedes yet never strays far,
follows yet never arrives late,
acts in stillness yet is inaudible,
advances and retreats unseen.
From beginning to end, fortune and misfortune,
auspiciousness and calamity,
all return as they ought—
none can escape beyond Heaven’s reach.
Heaven’s net is vast and wide,
though sparse, it never misses.

The people do not fear death—
they regard their lives lightly.
How can fear of death restrain them?
If the people were made to always fear death,
they would find peace.
But those who act strangely become blessings for others—
I could seize and execute them,
punish their deceitful excesses,
and impose stern laws with trustworthy punishments.
Yet who would dare?

There are always those who execute punishment—
*[referring to]* the great ministers.
And those who substitute for them—
*[referring to]* the sovereign.
This is improper—
the sovereign should not replace the great ministers in judgment.
If the sovereign were to replace the great carpenter in cutting,
his hands would soon be injured.
*[Annotation: "指归" (zhǐguī) here may imply a moral or practical conclusion.]*

Human nature and disposition:
the ignorant are faithful and trustworthy,
the knowing are shameless and deceitful;
content when fortunate,
scheming when misfortune strikes;
despising death in poverty,
cherishing life in ease.»

「How does one understand this? Zhuangzi says: The infant is faithful and trustworthy toward its enemies and rivals when it knows nothing, but when it grows strong and gains knowledge, it deceives even its elder brothers and sisters-in-law.

When the three armies are in high spirits, they abandon even captured soldiers; beasts in the narrow valleys do not flee from rhinoceroses or tigers. This is not because their nature has changed—it is simply the way of things.

From this, we see: if the people’s hearts are not won, their lives and fates are incomplete, then decrees and commands cannot move them. If they are wretched and distressed, they do not fear death lightly, then punishments and penalties cannot terrify them.

Thus, rulers who delight in knowledge, burdened by worldly cares and the people’s labor, sacrifice to heaven and earth, ward off calamities and dispel misfortunes, summon goodness and invite blessings, pray to spirits and deities, and manipulate all affairs—ever shifting to please the people—will find that households know kindness, streets abound with virtuous and wise men.

But if they defy the natural order, rely solely on human hearts, take their own opinions as wisdom, and their own arguments as just—if they abandon proper distinctions, disregard the principles of things, and obstruct the Dao while fostering false praise—then the world will be thrown into great chaos, the common folk will be restless and anxious, labor will be unending, and poverty will breed deceit.

Those who dare to defy will be honored and rewarded, the lighthearted will thrive in ease, the virtuous will suffer in want, those who fear the law will starve in distress, the weak will grow bitter and long for death, while the powerful will revel in their dominion over the king.

Thus, when the world rushes toward fame and contests for influence, without regard for right or wrong, splitting hairs and parsing minutiae, and treats death as a return home—then, if stern laws and harsh punishments are imposed, it is like restraining what is easy and controlling what is light. When it comes to this, people no longer fear death—how can one frighten them with it? Such words are senseless.

Hence, executions and punishments multiply, yet deceit does not cease; rewards and stipends are given, yet the great ministers do not obey; the common people do not rally, and the feudal lords do not submit. The land is not truly his, nor is the people’s loyalty. Therefore, the sage-ruler guides the people thus: the sovereign does nothing, yet the people have no grievances; the people gain nothing, yet the sovereign loses nothing.

His achievements are easily won yet hard to lose; his transformations are difficult to resist yet easy to enact; his garments are easily made yet hard to wear out; his food is easily sufficient yet hard to exhaust. Thus, those who crave life and abhor death, who shun excess and despise ruin, all hold their spirits dear and cherish their bodies, so that form can be controlled and influence can be restrained.

People fear death and value life—this is their natural state. Hence, even the bold and the wise, those who are open and far-reaching, do not dare to create blessings or wield authority. Thus, laws are established yet unused, rewards are set yet not bestowed.

Why is this so? Because the sovereign’s weight is as great as heaven and earth, while all other things are as light as feathers. If laws are stern and punishments severe, the wise know not to transgress. Those who alone do not fear, when their crimes are exposed, will be put to death by the officials—who would dare to defy?

Therefore, the Dao of the Emperor-King is to act without interference, to see without gazing, to hear without listening, to scheme without plotting, and to speak without uttering words. Before, behind, left, and right—each has its appointed duty. He relies on their responses to oversee, ensures their success, grants them their aspirations, and accepts their deeds. Titles are bestowed upon worthy lords, stipends given to advancing virtuous men. Those who create blessings will die, those who usurp authority will see their clans destroyed, and all officials will hasten to their duties—leaving the sovereign uninvolved.

Each has its proper role, none may overstep or substitute. When entrusted with what they are capable of, the Great Dao is achieved, all affairs become clear, and the realm stands still in perfect order, conquering all without effort.

If ministers act as sovereigns, they will lose their own lives; if sovereigns act as ministers, they will surely harm their states.

The *Dao/Way* and *Virtue/Power* of the True Classic’s *Annotations* [卷之十二] conclude here:

1. *威* ["威"] was originally *"为"* ["for"], corrected according to the *Secret Edition*.
2. *"所"* ["that which"] was originally omitted, added according to the *Secret Edition*.
3. *"常处"* ["constant abode"] was originally *"堂处"* ["hall abode"], corrected according to the *Secret Edition*.

The sovereign’s Dao lies in the unseen, the minister’s in the seen. The sovereign alone holds absolute authority, the ministers alone establish proper names. When the sovereign and ministers are separated, all affairs become clear.

The division between ruler and subject must never be blurred. Thus, when the sovereign’s position is fixed and the ministers’ duties are clear, all affairs become manifest. The sovereign has his allotted sphere, the ministers their appointed tasks. To discern their proper roles and duties is to ensure none may substitute for another. Each guards his own circle, the Great Dao is then achieved, all affairs become clear, and the realm stands still in perfect order, conquering all without effort.

If ministers act as sovereigns, they will lose their own lives; if sovereigns act as ministers, they will surely harm their states.

The *Dao/Way* and *Virtue/Power* of the True Classic’s *Annotations* conclude here.」

「#4 Weakness: Secret Edition reads *„nì* [溺] (indulgence).
#5 Security without knowing peril: Original reads *„ān shì bù zhī wēi* [安世不知危] (peace in the world without knowing peril), *„shì* [世] (world)“ superfluous; deleted per Secret Edition.
#6 Disaster cannot be wrought: Original omits *„bù néng huò* [不能祸] (cannot be wrought)“; supplemented per Yi Lan Edition.
#7 Each returns to its proper place: Original omits *„qí* [其] (its)“; supplemented per Yi Lan Edition.
#8 Fortune and misfortune are now manifest: Original omits *„zhuó yǐ* [着矣] (are now manifest)“; supplemented per Yi Lan Edition.

**The True Classic’s Dao/Way: Annotations, Volume Thirteen**
Composed by **Yán Zhēn of Shù Jùn** (严遵, *Yán Zūn*, *Yán Jūn Píng* 严君平), annotated by **Gǔ Shén Zǐ** (谷神子).

**Chapter on Human Hunger**
When humans hunger, they are never fully sated—
the sovereign’s excessive taxation is deemed a delight,
thus the people suffer from meager provisions.

The common folk are hard to govern, banditry arises—
because the sovereign’s actions are for himself alone,
the state thus falls into disorder and turmoil.

The people’s disregard for death is not of their own making—
their insatiable craving for life knows no bounds,
thus they lightly risk death and easily incur blame.

To not value life as equal to death is to be wiser than those who prize life’s ease and enduring comfort.

**Annotations:**
The Dao/Way’s bestowal upon humans has its measure,
the Heaven and Earth’s sustenance of humans has its measure,
the kings and nobles’ governance of the state has its measure,
the ministers’ fulfillment of their duties has its measure,
and all creatures’ preservation of their own lives has its measure.

Humans inherit life and are shaped by nature—
their hearts and minds are enlightened,
yet they gain life by their own accord.
The organs and viscera pass down in sequence,
*qi* and blood circulate in harmony,
surface and depth respond in unison,
high and low fulfill their roles,
flexion and extension move with ease,
sight and hearing are sharp and clear—
this is how the Dao/Way allots its measure to humans.

Heaven and Earth embrace and nurture—
clouds drift, rain falls,
thunder and wind act in concert,
the sun and moon alternate their courses,
spring blooms, summer flourishes,
autumn harvests, winter stores,
*yīn-yáng* (阴阳) in perfect accord,
all things thrive in abundance,
and humans act in ways that sustain life—
this is how Heaven and Earth allot their measure to humans.

Following the Dao/Way to cultivate *virtue/power*,
conforming to Heaven’s natural order,
exerting one’s utmost effort without ceasing,
embracing sincerity and practicing simplicity,
returning to the boundless *Wu Jí* (无极),
refining desires and paring excess,
setting an example for all under Heaven,
each upholding their proper name,
all perfecting their *virtue/power*,
enjoying life and securing the people,
the Four Seas in submission—
this is how kings and nobles safeguard their rule.

Great harmony and rectitude prevail,
uprightness without deviation,
loyalty and sincerity in obedience,
fulfilling their appointed duties,
approving the good and condemning the evil,
never altering truth for name,
not slighting their rank,
not demeaning their attire—
this is how ministers preserve their integrity.

With humble hearts and earnest simplicity,
practicing moderation and fortifying resolve,
conforming to Heaven’s seasons,
exhausting Earth’s resources,
wearing clothes suited to their form,
eating to harmonize their stomachs,
laboring at dawn and resting at dusk,
not despising their dwelling,
not wearying of their food—
this is how the common folk preserve their lives.

When movement and stillness lose harmony, they lose the Dao/Way’s measure;
When plowing and harvesting fall out of season, they lose Heaven’s measure;
When they abandon duty and follow their own will, they lose the sovereign’s measure;
When they fabricate and deceive, they lose the ministers’ measure;
When their clothing and food are ill-suited, they lose the people’s measure.

To lose the Dao/Way’s measure is to corrupt one’s nature;
To lose Heaven’s measure is to bring ruin to one’s family;
To lose the sovereign’s measure is to imperil the state;
To lose the ministers’ measure is to endanger one’s life;
To lose the people’s measure is to forfeit one’s existence.

The Dao/Way, Heaven and Earth, sovereign and ministers, officials and commoners—
their actions in motion and stillness each have their proper measure.
To uphold their measure is to ensure both high and low flourish;
To abandon their proper roles is to bring hardship to all.

To guard one’s measure as it should be is to align with Heaven and Earth,
to diminish oneself and spare excess is to walk with the Dao/Way.
If sacrifices are never ceased,
future generations will prosper.

To overstep one’s measure and seek excess
brings ill fortune upon oneself,
burdens that accumulate,
and calamity for future generations.

This is why ancient sages abandoned their bodies,
enduring hunger, cold, hardship, and poverty—
because their actions in motion and stillness were unharmonious,
their plowing and harvesting fell out of season,
they indulged their emotions and followed their desires,
their cravings for pleasure knew no limit,
they exhausted the five flavors in food,
adorned themselves with elaborate patterns,
pursued extravagance and licentiousness,
ignored the awe of Heaven,
amassed great labor and virtue,
yet could not restrain their excess.
Their consumption of food and clothing
was double that of ordinary people.

Thus, the people’s hunger stems
from the sovereign’s heavy taxation
and his failure to uphold his proper measure,
while they emulate his extravagance and waste.

Hence, the poor suffer in body,
their misfortunes extend to their descendants,
so rags are worn thin yet never discarded,
simple fare is lacking yet never spurned,
and the common folk secretly hoard gains while indulging in vice,
publicly neglecting duty while pursuing private interests.

Those who enrich their families grow wealthy,
those who scheme for the state grow poor,
the upright die first,
the honest suffer hardship and poverty,
customs decay and morals falter,
what is false prevails over truth,
chaos and disorder thrive,
virtuous men retreat in seclusion,
the weak and few are oppressed,
the strong and powerful dominate,
the poor become slaves,
the rich act like kings.

For they indulge in licentious rule and chaotic governance,
do not heed the natural order,
defy their own nature and yield to desire,
abandon the Dao/Way and stray from Heaven,
adorn false knowledge and fabricate schemes,
all to gratify their own hearts.

The people are hard to transform
because the sovereign acts unnaturally.

Thus, simplicity fades and purity vanishes,
crafty schemes multiply,
novelties abound daily,
beyond count or form,
flatterers are favored,
deceitful men are ennobled,
ministerial stratagems triumph,
the sovereign’s true Dao/Way is stifled,
paths of deceit and oppression are embellished,
dangers of annihilation and peril arise,
loyal and upright men are sidelined and grow ever distant,
while cunning deceivers gather in crowds and soar together.

The wicked do not die,
the corrupt are not punished,
power is established, influence prevails,
their authority shakes the armies—
whatever they gaze upon,
whatever they will,
comes swiftly to their command,
all for their own preservation,
yet they all exceed the bounds of the world,
some like this, some like that,
confused and discontented.

It is better to perish than to persist,
better to die than to live—
with bold resolve and reckless daring,
glowering eyes fixed upon one another,
sovereign and ministers conspire,
fathers and sons plot in secret,
boiling cauldrons cannot deter them,
sharp blades cannot stop them.

The people thus risk their lives for trifling gains,
seize opportunities in perilous straits,
defy heavy prohibitions,
charge into deadly blades,
brave flying arrows,
never heeding their own safety—
all for the sake of fleeting honor and excessive craving for life.»

The saying that people lightly risk death is because they crave life excessively. Thus, when they lose wealth or rank, some suffer gut-wrenching sorrow, drive themselves mad, hang themselves, waste their vital essence, abandon their spirit—constantly yearning for death, seizing blades to harm themselves, or plunging into deep waters. Hence, they hold death lightly.

Thus, the Way of Nature ever opposes things. The formless lives, the formed dies; those who pursue gain encounter calamity, those who seek fortune meet disaster. They come unsummoned, meet without invitation—I though I would avoid them, yet the world cannot escape. Therefore, the relentless pursuit of life and gain is the root of death, while abandoning form and rejecting gain is the root of life.

The nameless and lowly suffer no misfortune;
the humble and disgraced incur no blame;
coarse food and simple broth breed lasting contentment;
rough cloth and deer-hide garments bring enduring comfort;
when one does not burden the people, peace and ease endure;
when one does not cling to life, longevity follows.
This is the way to transcend life.

Thus, one who can abandon all selfish divisions, stripping away all desires, lies motionless like a corpse, stands unchanging like a wooden post—neither altered by transformation, nor distinguished by life and death, neither exalted as a sovereign, nor debased as a slave. Only in what one encounters, yielding to the unfolding of Nature, does one neither fear annihilation nor rejoice in life. Then one aligns with the Dao as a person, befriends Heaven and Earth, attains longevity and clear vision, cycles endlessly, and transcends all bounds of wealth and nobility—becoming the marketplace of the world. This surpasses the mere valuing of life.

**On the Nature of Life and Death**
Human life is soft and weak, for spirit resides in the body; death is rigid and strong, for spirit departs the body.
Grass and trees in life are soft and pliable, for yang-qi abides within; in death, they wither and decay, for yang-qi withdraws.

Thus, rigidity and strength are the form of death’s followers,
while softness and weakness are the bearing of life’s survivors.
A strong army cannot overcome its fourfold reversals;
a rigid tree cannot thrive among its kin.
Strength and grandeur bring ruin—this is the exhaustion of the Way;
smallness and weakness bring harmony—this is attaining the mean.

**The Pivot of Return:**
With all things it arises together, yet has no form or sound;
it lacks color or taste, nor does it reek or perfume.
It enters and exits without doors, comes and goes without gates—
above, it has no stalk; below, no root.
Serene and still, it never alters, preserving its constancy.
Harmonious and subtle, its transformations know no bounds.
It blends with all things, yet gives birth to the Three—
the origin of Heaven and Earth, the ancestral source of yin and yang.
When things exist, it abides; when things perish, it departs.
Unnameable, it is called *Shenming* [Divine Clarity].

Born of the Great Void, it thrives in the formless,
receiving without decay, bestowing without depletion.
In motion, it knows no end; in stillness, it is boundless.
It encompasses all without limit, penetrates all without exclusion—
flowing beyond all things, traversing all that exists.
Heaven gallops, Earth races, yet cannot overtake it;
yin strains, yang flees, yet cannot outstrip it.
Echoes exhaust themselves yet cannot respond;
shadows dissolve yet cannot resemble it.
Take from it, yet it is never spent;
give to it, yet it is never depleted.
It has no partiality in giving or taking,
no hatred or love—
it retains softness, dwells in weakness,
returning to emptiness.
Advancing and retreating, bending and stretching,
it ever aligns with *virtue/power*,
leading the Dao, while things grow distant.
Those who receive much are sage and wise;
those who receive little are foolish and dull.

Thus, the Divine Clarity and sage wisdom are the eternal masters of life;
softness, weakness, and stillness are the dwelling of Divine Clarity.
When spirit resides in the body, life is soft and weak—
this is the abode of Divine Clarity.

When Divine Clarity dwells in a person,
it cannot be removed if in its right place,
nor can it be retained if lost.
Neither coercive power nor clever schemes can control it.
If one can nurture it, wealth and nobility never fade,
and one remains forever in high station, growing ever more secure.

Thus, at birth, bones are weak, sinews supple,
blood and qi flow freely,
the mind is focused, spirit harmonious,
the face radiant, the body luminous,
movements graceful, every joint firm—
day by day, vitality increases, and within months, clarity sharpens.
Why? Because spirit resides within.

But as one ages, bones wither, sinews tighten,
hair turns white, flesh atrophies,
food and drink lose flavor,
hearing and sight dull,
strength and vigor wane daily,
movements decline monthly,
thought grows muddled, choices conflict.
At death, the body shrinks, the face hollows, the tongue retreats, the limbs stretch.
Why? Because spirit has departed.
This is the rigidity of death.

When grass and trees first sprout, branches are supple, leaves lush and green,
abundant and beautiful, soft and harmonious—
for yang-qi abides within.
As they decline, leaves yellow and wither, colors fade and perish.
At death, roots and stems wither, branches grow rigid and hard—
for yang-qi has departed.
Thus, grass and trees in life are soft and pliable, in death, withered and brittle.

Where Divine Clarity resides, the perilous may be secured, the dead revived;
where it departs, the safe may perish, the strong may be slain.
Where yang-qi abides, trees may bend and grass may bind;
where it flees, breath may congeal and ice may shatter.

Thus, Divine Clarity and yang-qi are the root of life,
while softness and weakness are the medicine for all things.
Harmony and yielding are the means of longevity,
the refuge of Divine Clarity and yang-qi.

All things follow yang in their yielding softness—
thus, rigidity and fullness are the likeness of death;
softness and smoothness are the dwelling of life.
The rigid and strong are death’s followers;
the soft and weak are life’s survivors.

In human nature, one hates a neighbor’s father for his strength,
loves a neighbor’s child for his weakness;
burns withered branches for their hardness,
adorns with jade and ornaments for their smoothness.
People love the young and hate the old—
for life’s vitality lies in their measure.

Thus, when there is no sovereign above, the feudal lords encroach upon one another,
contending states vie for power, armies are raised and poised to confront—
the soft and weak prevail, the rigid and strong are exhausted.

Why is this so?
The armies of the strong are not aligned with the Dao of Heaven and Earth;
their foundation is not in harmony and yielding, nor in virtue of compassion and love.
They punish the arrogant and violent, rescue the insufficient,
and break the greedy and the usurping.

The generals rely on the might of their state and the multitude of their people,
seeking glory and fame, mimicking the ways of others,
thus they covet others’ lands and wealth,
take joy in slaughter and injury,
ravage ancestral temples and shrines,
and bring ruin to the people—
all to display their overwhelming power.

Submissive and fearful below, they exploit others’ advantages to assert their own will,
those who draw near meet ruin, those who serve meet disaster,
their blame and cruelty run counter to human nature,
their slaughter defies the will of Heaven.

In life, they bring suffering to the world;
in death, they benefit the world.
The many, though weak, unite as one,
sharing sorrow and plotting together—
though they bear the name of strength,
in truth, they cannot prevail.

This is what is meant by *"strong armies do not conquer."*
Why is this so?
For they are bound by poisonous knots—
Heaven’s Dao does not favor them.

How can this be made clear?
In ancient times, mighty Qin and Chu
annihilated the feudal lords,
united the domains and cities,
grew rich in their states,
and became sovereigns, their power swaying the world,
their might trembling across the seas,
their honors and privileges reaching their peak—
they could be called strong indeed.

Yet, when they sat at ease, the feudal lords were in distress;
when they stretched their feet, the world trembled in fear;
when they issued commands, Heaven’s heart grieved;
when they took action, the Divine Clarity was disturbed—
their nations fell, their families were ruined,
and their own lives were cut short.

Why was this so?
Because they abandoned softness and weakness for rigidity and strength.

When the divine Han was about to rise,
they fled like dragons into hiding,
yet the people sought them out—
and they could not escape.

Father Heaven, Mother Earth,
loving the people as their own children,
rewarding merit and nurturing virtue,
teaching those who follow the Dao,
responding to enemies and adapting to change,
their strategies vast as rivers and seas—
they conquered Qin and extinguished Chu,
the world rejoiced in harmony,
and they were established as sovereigns.

Why was this so?
Because they acted with compassion and humility,
in accordance with Heaven’s will.

This is not only true of human affairs—
it applies to all things.

Great trees, towering a hundred *rèn* high,
with massive trunks and branches,
their heads split through the central gate,
their roots divided by the doorframe—
though they bear no fruit,
they endure at the lowest level.

Yet countless smaller trees,
stretching their limbs and leaves,
lush and verdant,
thrive above them—
for their forms are great yet their strength is weak.
This is what is meant by *"strong trees are shared."*

Thus, the Dao of Divine Clarity,
the principle of Heaven and Earth,
does not uphold the small to bear the great,
nor the light to bear the heavy.

Hence, the strong cannot rule as kings,
nor can the mighty trees dwell above.
The strong and mighty reside below;
the weak and small dwell above—
this is the natural order of things.
When the weak and small dwell above,
it is the fixed law of Heaven.

The **Chapter of Heaven’s Dao**:
Heaven’s Dao is both yin and yang,
like a drawn bow with its tension and release.
The high are lowered to grow long and strong,
the low are raised to begin their life.
Those with excess are diminished to break fullness,
those lacking are supplemented to fill emptiness.
Heaven’s Dao:
to diminish excess and balance is constant,
to supplement deficiency and provide support is to court ruin.

Human affairs are not so—
they cannot be thus practiced.
They diminish the deficient, plunder the weak,
and hoard the excess for the mighty.
Who can diminish the excess with clear insight,
and bestow the world’s blessings to all,
thus ensuring life and harmony together?
Only the one who follows the Dao is nameless.

Thus, the Sage says of the enlightened king:
*"He acts but does not rely on force,
he achieves success but does not dwell upon it,
he does not seek fame nor fear great calamity.
He points the way—*

Before Heaven and Earth began,
yin and yang had not yet emerged,
cold and heat had not yet appeared,
light and darkness had not yet formed—
there stood a primordial entity,
one turbid, one pure,
the pure above, the turbid below,
harmony in the center.
When these three arose together,
Heaven and Earth were complete,
yin and yang intermingled,
and all things were born.

Those who lose it perish,
those who grasp it flourish.
In things, harmony is:
rigid yet never broken,
soft yet never obstructed.
In Heaven, it is the measure;
on Earth, it is the standard.
In yang, it is the compass;
in yin, it is the square.
It does not move without restraint,
nor does it rest without purpose.
Things rely on softness and weakness,
while qi depends on firmness and strength—
in motion, it controls all,
in stillness, it accommodates all.

Thus, harmony is:
the application of virtue and power,
the support of Divine Clarity,
the mechanism of Heaven and Earth,
the dwelling of all living things,
the essential principle of the ten thousand directions,
the repository of nature,
the gateway of all blessings,
the door to universal fortune.

Hence, the wise see it as wisdom,
the virtuous see it as virtue—
the world clings to it day and night,
yet none can fully comprehend it.

Why is this so?
Because its power in nurturing things is subtle,
and its achievement of affairs is profound.

Thus, Heaven and Earth’s Dao:
does no harm nor injury,
its action is non-action—
it guards with great harmony and rectitude,
adapting to things with depth and sincerity,
upholding nothing in vain,
claiming nothing for itself—
each thing fulfills its nature,
and all things return to their natural state.

Hence, the flourishing self-destruct,
the stretched self-loosen,
the hidden self-reveal,
the obscure self-illuminate,
the deficient gain support,
the excessive are diminished—
those who exist will perish,
those who live will die,
right and wrong invert themselves,
fortune and misfortune are self-taken—
diminishment cannot be avoided,
gain cannot be resisted—
calamity does not linger constantly,
fortune does not remain forever—
each receives its due share,
and none can possess both.

Thus, the scaly have no fur,
the furred have no feathers,
the winged have no teeth,
the horned have no claws—
those seen in the day vanish at night,
those gained in the past are lost in the future,
double benefits are rare,
and never experienced before.

Affairs do not flourish in parallel,
benefits do not come in pairs.
Not too great, nor too small—
fixed in unity, unchanging—
once they reach their fullness,
they cannot be resolved.

「Thus, the sun reaches its zenith and then declines,
the moon waxes full and then wanes,
the seasons shift in their ceaseless change—
one wanes as the other waxes.

Beneath towering peaks, deep valleys must lie;
where great springs flow, fierce waves arise.
Molten gold and stone harden in frozen ice,
the earth’s fissures are shaped by yin and yang—
this is the Dao/Way of Heaven:
when yin reaches its extreme, yang ascends,
when yang reaches its extreme, yin descends.

All craftsmen follow this principle without fail.

Now, the archer’s craft:
when the bow is strung, it both contracts and expands,
when drawn, it both tightens and releases—
its shape is measured by compass and square,
its tension guided by the plumb line.
If the string is too high and taut, it is slackened;
if too low and loose, it is pulled upward.
Excess is trimmed, deficiency is supplemented;
the string’s balance is entrusted to its quality,
the bow’s harmony to its draw—
thus, when struck, the bow sends the arrow true.

Heaven’s Dao/Way is the same:
hence it is said, *"It is like drawing a bow."*

To elevate the low and suppress the high—
this is the Dao/Way of Heaven and Earth.
To act against Heaven’s will to please the people,
to act against the people’s will to follow the Dao/Way—
the virtuous serve as assistants,
the sage rules as master.
He strives to love with abundance,
to govern as both left and right hand.
The wise occupy high positions,
the foolish occupy low ones.
Great merit earns high rank,
minor talents earn low office.
Outstanding deeds receive meager rewards,
exhaustive labor yields heavy stipends—
this is the Dao/Way of the feudal lords.

Thus, the conduct of humanity.

To deceive the sincere and loyal,
to insult the faithful and upright,
to oppress the weak and rob the few,
to enslave the ignorant and burden the common folk,
to take from the slack and give to the taut,
to diminish the small and empower the strong,
to oppose what is obscure and favor what is manifest—
this is the Dao/Way of the many,
the so-called *"supplementing the deficient to serve the excessive."*

To dwell in the small while possessing the great,
to abide in the dim while holding the bright,
to stand firm while the weak are subdued,
to gather strength while the few are scattered,
to rise above while sinking low—
thus, the lofty cannot be cut down,
the full cannot be shattered,
the great cannot be broken,
the overflowing cannot be diminished,
the rigid cannot be bent,
the yielding cannot be blocked,
the solitary cannot be controlled,
the feeble cannot be seized,
the foolish cannot be belittled,
the empty cannot be filled—
Heaven and Earth protect them as their own children,
the people sustain them as their own mothers.
In harmony with all, they soar with the Dao/Way,
aligned with the Dao/Way, they begin and end with it—
Heaven and humanity in mutual accord,
the divine and the bright as their steadfast guardians—
this is the Dao/Way of the *至人* (perfected sage).

The perfected sage always lacks nothing for himself yet ever has abundance,
uniting with the Dao/Way to nurture all under Heaven—
thus, they are called *有道者* (those of the Dao/Way).

Therefore, the sage’s actions:
no fixed purpose, yet in perfect accord,
gentleness and weakness as their foundation,
hidden yet never exhausted,
honored yet never ostentatious—
they decline titles and reject wealth,
spare their strength and take nothing extra,
fill the land yet do not enter it,
nurture the people yet do not befriend them.
They tremble as if they had already lost something,
they vigilantly guard as if already guilty—
walking the Dao/Way in harmony,
constantly befriending all things,
mastering Heaven’s patterns,
penetrating Earth’s principles—
they achieve success yet do not claim it,
their virtue flows yet they do not hoard it.
This is the saying: *"Do and do not rely upon it; achieve success and do not claim it."*

They flee fame and evade power,
the mysterious and dark as their refuge—
they extinguish extremes and conceal traces,
the formless as their guardian—
in stillness and solitude, vast as empty space,
none can take from or give to them—
proud yet independent,
aligned with Heaven’s Dao/Way.

Why is this so?
They fear the calamities of the Dao/Way,
and dare not appear before the virtuous.

Though they possess merits that could overshadow Heaven,
they do not wish to be seen—
this is the saying: *"Not wishing to appear before the virtuous,"*
the conduct of sages and wise philosophers.

**Chapter on the Soft and Weak:**
Under Heaven, nothing is softer and weaker than water,
yet it erodes the hard and strong—
its power is like that of a whetstone or chisel.
None can precede water in its formation,
and nothing can surpass it—
this is its unparalleled nature.

Water’s victory over the strong lies in its ability to submerge all things,
the yielding overcoming the rigid yet never exhausted—
all under Heaven know its purpose clearly,
yet few can act with its scarcity and weakness.

The sage’s words say:
*"The pattern of transmitting the state"*—
to endure the state’s filth and shame,
to bear its disgrace—
this is called the ruler of the feudal lords, the *侯伯* (marquis and earl).
To endure the state’s misfortunes and calamities,
to bear its ills—
this is called the ruler of all under Heaven, the *帝王* (emperor and king).
Straight words appear as contrary,
actions seem to clash with their purpose.

To pacify great grudges and offenses,
if the ruler harms the people,
there will be even greater resentment toward the virtuous—
how can this be considered good?
Thus, the sage says of the *明君* (enlightened ruler):
they hold the left contract but do not demand it of others—
this is the Dao/Way of nature.

The virtuous govern through contracts,
seeking virtue within themselves;
the unvirtuous govern through tracks,
demanding it from others.

Heaven’s Dao/Way has no favorites—
it is just and harmonious,
its virtue always aligning with the good.

**Conclusion:**
Moral principles encompass all,
Heaven and Earth bear them,
yin and yang transform them,
the sun and moon illuminate them—
all things flourish together,
in chaotic yet ordered unity,
their rise and fall, existence and demise,
changing with the times.

The rigid accumulate and thus fail,
the yielding endure and thus prevail—
this is the natural law of all things.

Hence, water’s ability to strike stone and pierce metal,
to topple mountains and breach dikes,
to flow ceaselessly in cycles of ebb and tide,
to sink into boundlessness—
vast and endless,
far-reaching with no shore,
its clarity cannot be obscured,
its strength cannot be augmented,
though murky, it can become pure,
though few, it can multiply—
in peril, it can restore safety,
in haste, it can bring repose—
changing with the times,
it dies yet is reborn.

It permeates all things,
nurtures all forms—
it bestows without depletion,
sustains without decay—
it pours into the four seas yet claims no merit,
it matches Heaven and Earth yet does nothing extra—
within the minutest spaces, it moves with ease,
above the nine wilds, it soars without effort—
its blessings reach the azure heavens,
its depths accumulate in the yellow earth—
it strengthens to support Heaven and Earth,
it weakens to sink beneath fur and feathers,
it gathers the sparse to form rivers and seas.
High and low have no fixed order,
it begins and ends without end—
advancing, retreating, bending, stretching—
this is the nature of those near the Dao/Way,
for its form is soft and weak,
its actions and stillness await the times,
it does not set a fixed direction,
its harmony is smooth and effortless.

Thus, all craftsmen,
though their works differ in purpose and method,
rely on water’s power—
to soften gold and stone,
to break the hard and bend the rigid,
to level the low and refine the subtle,
to release the bound and ease all forms,
to blend the five flavors,
to purify foul odors,
to strike the firm and enter the great—
none can do this without water’s aid.»

The things beneath Heaven have none so soft and weak as water, yet none can surpass it in overcoming the strong.

Why is this so? All things are skilled in their own ways, yet none can rival it.

Thus, water extinguishes fire,
grinds and sharpens metal,
shatters horns and teeth,
yet harms no tongue or ear.
It humbles itself to conquer the proud and violent,
endures insult to triumph over great grudges—
all know this, all hear of it,
yet none act accordingly, for they follow their private desires without end.

Water’s mastery over strength,
softness over hardness—
all know it, yet none can practice it.

Thus, speaking brings ruin,
silence brings harm,
advancing invites calamity,
retreating invites blame.
Labor without virtue,
toil without reward—
brightness fades into obscurity,
long endurance sinks into darkness,
greatness turns to smallness,
preservation leads to ruin.

Therefore, enlightened kings and sage rulers, when inheriting a state,
must make solemn vows:
to bear the nation’s filth,
to be its sovereign;
to endure the nation’s misfortunes,
to be its king of all.

This is what the sage said.
What is meant by bearing the nation’s filth?
It means:
to eat what the people spit out,
to wear what the people discard,
to dwell where the people are sent,
to find joy in the people’s suffering,
to strive only in serving the people,
never pausing to attend to oneself.

Thus, the people entrust them as a father,
love them as a mother,
willing to be their servants,
to die with them together.
Therefore, even in barren lands of cold and stone,
remote and desolate regions,
they turn humiliation into honor,
preserve their ancestral line,
transform calamity into blessing,
and endure as the people’s lord.
This is called bearing the nation’s filth,
and thus they become the sovereign of the state.

What is meant by enduring the nation’s misfortunes?
It means:
to endure what the people despise,
to bear what the people hate,
to face the people’s greatest calamities,
yet not consider it virtue,
to plan for the nation’s ruin,
yet not lose Heaven’s favor,
to consider death,
yet not lose the people’s blessing—
Heaven and Earth bow with them,
all creatures rise and fall with them,
they follow east and west,
attach to south and north.
Where they act, ruin follows;
where they scheme, submission comes.
The world unites as one domain,
all turn toward the wind and embrace transformation,
none fail to receive its blessings.

Thus, they can rectify the crooked and pacify chaos,
conquer without resistance,
transform decadence into simplicity,
achieve great virtue and boundless renown.
This is called enduring the nation’s misfortunes,
and thus they become the king of all.

Why is this so?
Because their hearts are soft, their wills weak,
they value others above themselves,
and find ease in humiliation.

Thus, upright speech seems contrary,
yet none can overturn it;
near yet seems distant,
yet none can fathom it;
seeking it from afar,
yet none can attain it—
all lies within oneself.

How so?
Luxury lies within oneself,
yet they appear frugal to others;
wickedness lies within themselves,
yet they seek righteousness from the people;
disaster is invited by themselves,
yet they pray for blessings to Heaven—
Heaven and the spirits show them,
yet they cannot see;
the spirits warn them,
yet they cannot hear.

Abandoning this, they turn to harmony of heart,
their actions here resonate with Heaven,
rejecting themselves while blaming others—
the world falls into great confusion.

They rely on clear laws,
execute punishments with sincerity,
their words and deeds are trustworthy,
rewards and punishments are just,
public and impartial—
yet they exceed Heaven’s intent,
uprightness without deceit—
yet they lose the people’s hearts.
When punishments and executions are used together,
evil only grows;
when rewards are deep and gifts heavy,
chaos only increases.

At such times,
the virtuous are ensnared,
the wise fall under law—
though they gain reputation and substance,
how can it be good?

This is called pacifying great grudges,
yet leaving lingering resentment—
how can it be called good?

Thus, the sage holds the Dao’s token,
grasps virtue’s sincerity,
and unites it within themselves,
never demanding it of others.

Therefore, a ruler of virtue,
when they wish to act,
must consult Heaven first;
when they wish to proceed,
must verify the token’s truth.
They seek faults within themselves,
never blame the people;
they accept calamity upon themselves,
never resent others.
Thus, the sage holds the left contract
and does not demand accountability from others.

Therefore, right and wrong are self-determined,
black and white are self-divided—
before they act, the world responds;
before they command, all things comply.
Virtue governs the contract.

The unvirtuous,
pursuing their own desires,
ignoring the people’s needs,
lose governance and invite chaos,
seek no reflection in themselves,
yet blame the people’s failures,
enforce harsh punishments—
when people have faults,
families bear guilt,
the populace seethes with resentment,
Heaven’s will is unfulfilled,
their nation falls into disorder,
and future generations suffer.
The unvirtuous govern the traces.

Thus, the Dao of Heaven and Earth
moves with all things,
neither favoring nor rejecting,
neither distant nor close—
responding in kind, like echo to sound,
stillness and motion in harmony, like shadow to form,
neither crooked nor flattering,
upright as constant—
evil meets negation,
goodness meets passage,
softness and weakness find accord,
non-action aligns with harmony.

Heaven’s Dao has no favorites,
yet it always sides with the virtuous—
truth is never empty.

**Chapter on Small States and Few People**
Small states, few people—
narrow and few in number.

Let people have ten or a hundred measures of grain,
wealth and treasures unused—
let them practice frugality.
Let them value death,
find fulfillment in their place—
let them not wander far,
find contentment in their homeland.

Though they have carriages and boats,
their wealth is unused—
the people are simple and unadorned.
Though they have armor and arms in store,
they have no need to display them—
great harmony arises.

Let them use knotted cords for records,
returning to ancient simplicity.
Their food is plain, without refined flavors;
their clothing is plain, without fine colors;
their customs are convenient, aligned with the Dao;
their dwellings are secure, self-sustaining.

Neighboring states are visible,
yet each guards their own—
the sounds of roosters and dogs are heard,
yet few are true friends.
People live to old age,
fulfilling their natural span,
rarely traveling or visiting others.

**Conclusion:**
States vary in size,
lands in difficulty,
people in number,
wealth in abundance,
forms in height and low,
paths in distance,
strength in power,
authority in weight.

The great overcome the small,
the easy overcome the difficult,
the rich overcome the poor,
the many overcome the few,
the high overcome the low,
the near overcome the far,
the strong overcome the weak,
the light overcome the heavy—
this is the natural order of things.

Strength and weakness depend on the general,
safety and peril rest with the minister,
gain and loss lie with the ruler,
life and death hinge on the Dao.

Heaven does not always favor,
the people do not always dwell in one place—
those of virtue gather to them,
those without virtue are abandoned—
this is the natural Way.

Thus, vast lands and numerous people,
brave generals and stern rulers,
are insufficient to ensure strength;
sturdy armor and seasoned troops,
high walls and deep moats,
are insufficient to ensure safety;
persuasive speech and flattering words,
false compliance and fawning,
are insufficient to earn loyalty;
ceding territory and offering treasures,
forming alliances or counter-alliances,
gathering factions and thriving in power,
are insufficient to ensure survival.

Only those who embody the Dao
can master all things—
with virtue profound and grace abundant,
they turn the small into the great,
the weak into the strong,
the light into the heavy,
the few into the many.

Thus, the virtuous ruler, though in a small state,
must necessarily endure;
the unvirtuous ruler, though in a great state,
shall inevitably perish.

Therefore, the ruler of a small state,
with narrow lands and few people,
shallow virtue and light authority,
unregarded by neighboring lords,
whose laws fail to restrain,
who lacks natural barriers of hills and rivers,
nor the protection of allied states,
isolated and alone among great powers—
whose land is barren and unworthy of sacrifice,
whose treasures are meager and unworthy of offering,
whose generals and ministers do not align,
whose people are easily swayed,
whose neighbors seek to exploit—
such a state, without the steadfastness of a mountain,
nor the security of a highland,
dwells perpetually in the peril of a stacked egg.

Yet to attack it would not be tyranny,
nor would its virtue be excessive—
for the small state is the hinge of ruin and the pivot of peace.

The ruler of a small state, prone to peril and defeat,
must cultivate humility and the Dao,
daily increasing his virtue—
thus it is called the hinge of ruin and the pivot of peace.

Therefore, the sage who governs a small state
transforms misfortune into fortune,
converts danger into security.

Let there be abundance in boats and chariots,
let there be stores of armor and arms,
let tools and implements be convenient,
let food and clothing be plentiful,
let cattle and horses multiply,
let granaries overflow—
yet tenfold the wealth of neighboring states,
to secure the people’s hearts.

Though capable, do not act;
though wise, do not scheme;
let flavors be simple, unrefined,
let kitchens be plain, undecorated;
sever sensory indulgence, reject ornamentation—
let the ruler set the example,
drink before eating,
labor before resting,
wear a single layer in summer,
a double layer in winter,
seeking only harmony and ease,
not indulging in fine garments,
laboring only for life’s necessities,
not for gluttony—
let taxes be light and few,
so the people have surplus strength.

Cut off the roots of aggression,
and let the flow of wealth increase—
let customs grow sincere and frugal,
let loyalty and sincerity flourish,
let abundance seem like scarcity,
let capability seem like deficiency.

This is how the virtuous ruler
makes a small state with few people
possess tenfold wealth yet not use it.

Why is this so?

Establish it with the Dao,
embrace it with virtue,
share labor and ease, danger and security,
with the people as one.

Let the heart be balanced and harmonious,
listen to verify substance,
speak with reverence and humility,
to exalt the spirit,
let wisdom and virtue shine,
to correct errant tendencies—
then neighboring states will not stir,
the people will be united and content,
delighting in life and secure in longevity,
avoiding banditry and crime.

At such times, without bells and drums,
all things are sufficient,
the people are in harmony,
and ruler and subjects are in accord.
Attached to their land and content with life,
they die in their native hills and valleys.
Even if offered a hundredfold gain,
they will not abandon their homeland—
this is how one makes death precious
and prevents emigration.

Families have boats and chariots,
yet no need to transport or ride them;
households possess armor and arms,
yet no need to wield or display them.
Though boats and chariots exist,
they are not used;
though armor and arms exist,
they are not marshaled.

Why is this?

「Generals and ministers clearly understand the Dao/Way of the ruler,
the people diligently till and weave,
accumulating wealth as their aim.
When the ruler embodies humility and simplicity,
softness and weakness are esteemed,
loyalty and respect are prized,
while cleverness is held low and labor is valued.
With laws clear and customs settled,
superior and inferior mutually safeguard each other,
orders need not be given yet the people comply,
battles need not be fought yet enemies tremble.
Those who seek profit do not debate the difficulty of victory,
those who chase fame do not scheme to avoid blame.

Thus, the realm stands alone in tranquility,
the people undisturbed.
Discarding artifice and embracing sincerity,
they return to the simplicity of primordial times.

With knotted cords they mark the seasons,
in unadorned hearts they speak of truth,
simplifying things and refining words—
few in speech, few in schemes—
all in accord with Nature,
each finding their proper place.

This is what is called
*restoring the knotted cords* while using them.

Plain food and coarse gruel,
lacking flavor yet deemed sweet;
homespun cloth and deer-hide garments,
without patterns yet deemed fine.

Sweet their food, fine their garments.
Steep cliffs and perilous paths,
though treacherous, bring peaceful repose;
remote valleys and distant lands,
